CHAPTER III THE DUTIES, DISABILITIES AND PRIVILEGES OF THE VARá¹†AS

The duties and privileges of {{vará¹‡as|vará¹‡as}} occupy a very prominent place in all works on DharmaÅ›Ästra. The study (of the Vedas), offering sacrifices and giving gifts are said to be the duties absolutely enjoined on the brÄhmaá¹‡a, ká¹£atriya and vaiÅ›ya,1 while each of these three vará¹‡as has certain peculiar privileges, which are its principal means of livelihood. {{Teach iog|Teaching}} Vedas, officiating at sacrifices and receiving giftsâ€”these are the privileges of brÄhmaá¹‡as; the profession of arms and protection of the people are the {{peouliar|peculiar}} privileges of ká¹£atriyas; agriculture, rearing cattle, trade and {{inoney-lending|money-lending}} are the peculiar privileges of vaiÅ›yas. The first three viz. study, sacrifices and liberality are said to be the dharmas of all dvijas and the other actions such as teaching the Veda are said to be the vá¹›tti or {{jivikÅ«|jÄ«vikÄ}} (means of livelihood) of the dvijas. The consequence of this bifurcation is that if the first three are not performed or are {{negleoted|neglected}}, the person concerned was deemed to incur {{sia|sin}}, while a brÄhmaá¹‡a is not bound necessarily to earn his livelihood by all or any one of the three viz. teaching, officiating at a sacrifice or receiving gifts.2 A few words on each of these duties and privileges must be said at this stage.

Study of the Veda

Study (of Vedas). It has been already stated (p. 38) that brÄhmaá¹‡a and learning had become indissolubly connected even in early Vedic periods. We see in the Åšatapatha Br. and in the Upaniá¹£ads that certain kings had attained eminence as philosophers or students of {{brahma-vidya|brahmavidyÄ}} and then even learned brÄhmaá¹‡as came to them as pupils. For example, {{YÄjÄavalkya|YÄjÃ±avalkya}} learnt from Janaka (Åšat. Br. XI. 6. 2. 1-5), BÄlÄki GÄrgya from [[106]] AjÄtaÅ›atru, king of KÄÅ›Ä« (Bá¹›. Up. II. 1 and Kauá¹£Ä«taki Up. IV), {{Svetaketu Arunoya|Åšvetaketu Ä€ruá¹‡eya}} from PravÄhaá¹‡a3 Jaivali (ChÄn. Up. V. 3), five brÄhmaá¹‡as from AÅ›vapati, king of Kekaya (ChÄn. V. 11). In Bá¹›. Up. (IV. 2. 1) Janaka is described by {{YÄjÅ¾avalkya|YÄjÃ±avalkya}} as one who had studied the Vedas and Upaniá¹£ads.4 From this it may be inferred that some ká¹£atriyas at least spent a good deal of time in the study of religious and philosophical doctrines. The conclusion5 that is sometimes [[107]] drawn by certain writers that ká¹£atriyas or kings were the pioneers in brahmavidyÄ cannot be accepted as correct.

The germs of the philosophy of the Upaniá¹£ads are seen in the later hymns of the á¹šgveda, in the Atharvaveda and in some of the BrÄhmaá¹‡a treatises. The Upaniá¹£ads are full of brÄhmaá¹‡as who independently propounded various aspects of {{brahma-vidya|brahmavidyÄ}} and there is no reason to suppose that the few ká¹£atriyas referred to as masters of the vidyÄ were the only persons who first attained to that position. There are hardly any ancient passages to show that vaiÅ›yas devoted any portion of their time to veda study. The KÄá¹­haka-saá¹ƒhitÄ6 (IX. 16) indicates that all vará¹‡as studied the Veda since it speaks of a person not a brÄhmaá¹‡a, having studied (Vedic) lores and yet not shining (by his learning).

As to brÄhmaá¹‡as the matter stands thus. The Nirukta (II. 4) contains four7 verses (which are called {{VidyÄsÄkta|VidyÄsÅ«kta}}), the first of which says that vidyÄ came to the brÄhmaá¹‡a and requested him to guard her as his treasure. The MahÄbhÄá¹£ya8 of PataÃ±jali quotes as an Ägama (Vedic passage) the words ‘a brÄhmaá¹‡a should study and understand without any motive (of profit) dharma, the Veda with its six subsidiary lores (viz. phonetics etc.)’. Manu IV. 147 says ‘a brÄhmaá¹‡a should always and assiduously study the Veda alone; that (Veda study) is his highest dharma; everything else is inferior dharma’. YÄj. (I. 198) observes ’the Creator created brÄhmaá¹‡as for the preservation of the Vedas, for the satisfaction of the [[108]] gods and pitá¹›s, for the safeguarding of dharma.’ Atri (25) contains the same idea. Other9 sages say that he in whose family Veda (Vedic study) and vedi (consecration of fires for Å›rauta rites) are given up for three generations becomes a durbrÄhmaá¹‡a (an unworthy or bad brÄhmaá¹‡a). The Tai. S. (II. 1. 10. 1) prescribes a rite for a durbrÄhmaá¹‡a in whose family cessation of drinking soma occurred for generations and who himself desired to drink soma.

Teaching the Veda

Teaching the Veda. It is probable that in very remote times the son was taught the Veda by his father. The story of Åšvetaketu Ä€ruá¹‡eya (ChÄndogya V. 3. 1 and VI. 1. 1-2 and Bá¹›. Up. VI. 2. 1) shows that he learnt all the Vedas from his father and the legend in the Bá¹›. Up. (V. 2. 1) that the gods, men and asuras learnt from their father PrajÄpati points in the same direction.10 á¹šg. VII. 103. 5 shows that instruction was oral and consisted in the pupil repeating the words uttered by the teacher.11 Whatever may have been the case in very remote times, from the times of the BrÄhmaá¹‡a literature and in the times of the dharmaÅ›Ästras teaching Vedic literature was almost universally in the hands of brÄhmaá¹‡as. Some ká¹£atriya teachers or philosophers are referred to in the Åšatapatha (VIII. 1. 4. 10 and XI. 6. 2) and elsewhere, but they are generally held in low esteem. The Ä€p. Dh. S.12 (II. 2. 4. 25-28) lays down that the brÄhmaá¹‡a alone can be the teacher (of a brÄhmaá¹‡a), but in distress (i.e. in the absence of a brÄhmaá¹‡a teacher), a brÄhmaá¹‡a may learn from a ká¹£atriya or vaiÅ›ya, but the only service (which as a pupil) he should render to a ká¹£atriya or vaiÅ›ya teacher is to go after him (and not shampooing his feet etc.) and after the brÄhmaá¹‡a finishes his study, the brÄhmaá¹‡a may go in front [[109]] (of his ká¹£atriya teacher). Gaut. (VII. 1-3) and Manu (X. 1, II. 241) lay down the same rules. Manu (II. 242) adds that a perpetual student (naiá¹£á¹­hika brahmacÄrÄ«) should not stay as a pupil with a teacher who is not a brÄhmaá¹‡a and that a brÄhmaá¹‡a may learn even from a Å›Å«dra a useful or efficacious craft (II. 238).

The profession of teaching the Veda could not have brought much money or wealth to brÄhmaá¹‡as, since very great emphasis was laid, as we shall see later, on teaching without any prior agreement about payment. It was the privilege of a brÄhmaá¹‡a alone to officiate as a priest. Jaimini13 says that, as the ká¹£atriya and vaiÅ›ya cannot be priests (á¹›tviks), the sattra (a sacrifice extending over many days or years) could be performed only by brÄhmaá¹‡as. The KÄtyÄyana-Å›rautasÅ«tra uses a similar argument. When ViÅ›vÄmitra agreed to perform a sacrifice for TriÅ›aá¹…ku who had been cursed to be a cÄá¹‡á¸Äla, the RÄmÄyaá¹‡a says that the gods and sages would not accept the oblations.14 It is doubtful whether the same rigid rule prevailed in ancient Vedic days. In á¹šg. X. 98. 7 it is said that DevÄpi was the purohita of Åšantanu and the Nirukta (II. 10) adds that DevÄpi and Åšantanu were brothers and descendants of Kuru. So, according to the Nirukta at least, a ká¹£atriya could be a purohita in Vedic times. It may be admitted that the á¹šg. itself does not expressly say that they were brothers. In modern times many writers often speak of brÄhmaá¹‡as as the priestly caste or as priests. But this is not a very accurate statement. All brÄhmaá¹‡as never were nor are priests; besides even in modern times when caste is so rigid all priests in all temples and shrines are not brÄhmaá¹‡as. Some brÄhmaá¹‡as became the family priests (purohitas) of kings, many engaged as á¹›tviks at solemn Å›rauta sacrifices or at domestic rites and ceremonies. Temple priests are comparatively a later institution and they were generally looked down upon in olden times and are regarded as inferior even in modern times.15 Manu (III. 152) says that a devalaka i.e. a brÄhmaá¹‡a who took [[110]] remuneration to perform service before the image in a temple for three years continuously was unfit to be invited at a Å›rÄddha or to officiate in a sacrifice for gods.

Accepting gifts

The third means of livelihood permitted to brÄhmaá¹‡as was receiving gifts from a worthy or unblemished person. According to Yama16 quoted in the Sm. C. (I. p. 179) pratigraha (receiving gifts) from a worthy person of the three higher vará¹‡as is superior to the acquisition of wealth by officiating as a priest or by teaching. But Manu (X. 109-111) says that pratigraha from an unworthy person (or a Å›Å«dra) is worse than the act of teaching him or officiating as a priest for him. Very elaborate rules were laid down about gifts i.e. who should receive gifts, from whom gifts may be received and on what occasions and what things were proper subjects of gift. The latter two matters will be discussed in detail later on. Here the rules about the persons to whom gifts should be made and from whom they were to be accepted will be set out. It appears from the Bá¹›. Up. (IV. 1. 3) that even in those ancient times there were prohibitions against receiving gifts from unworthy persons and officiating as priests for the unworthy. And the Bá¹›. Up. (V. 14. 5-6) suggests that it is only the learned who could properly accept large gifts.

In the first place, the ideal set before brÄhmaá¹‡as was one of poverty, of plain living and high thinking, of forsaking the active pursuit of riches and cherishing cultural preservation and advancement. Manu lays down the general rule that when not in distress a brÄhmaá¹‡a should acquire wealth only just sufficient to maintain himself and his family, and to enable him to perform his religious duties without causing any harm to others or by as little harm to others as possible and without unduly worrying his own body (IV. 2-3) and then Manu (IV. 7-8) says17 that a brÄhmaá¹‡a householder may [[111]] either accumulate so much grain (but not more) as would fill a kusÅ«la (a granary), or a kumbhÄ«18 or he may have as much corn as would satisfy all his needs for three days or as much as will suffice for the day that is on and that out of these four each succeeding one was superior to each preceding one i.e. one who had no more accumulation of material goods than for the day itself (and who did not care for the morrow) was the best brÄhmaá¹‡a. YÄj. I. 128 speaks of a fifth grade viz. a brÄhmaá¹‡a should subsist by collecting the ears of corn that are left in the field after the crops are gathered or the single grains of corn so left and Manu (X. 112) says that if a brÄhmaá¹‡a cannot maintain himself he may prefer to live on the collection of fallen ears of corn or grains rather than receive gifts. This last mode is designated á¹›ta by Manu (IV. 5). Manu (IV. 12, 15, 17) lays down that a brÄhmaá¹‡a should cultivate supreme contentment and though desirous of happiness should restrain himself (in the pursuit of wealth), he should not, even when in distress, hanker after the acquisition of wealth by excessive attachment or by doing what is forbidden or by accepting gifts from any person whatever (of blemished character etc.) and he should give up pursuits that are opposed to (cause obstacles in) his devotion to Vedic study. YÄj. (I. 129) says the same thing in more concise language. VyÄsa prescribes that a brÄhmaá¹‡a should seek to narrow down his means of livelihood and should not hanker after excess of wealth; if he sets about accumulation of wealth he loses the (glorious) status of brÄhmaá¹‡ya.19 The MahÄbhÄrata says that when a brÄhmaá¹‡a has more corn than he would require to satisfy his needs for three years, then he should offer a sacrifice with that wealth and he should not go on accumulating wealth in vain and that accumulation of vast wealth is a calamity for a brÄhmaá¹‡a.20

Gautama (IX. 63), YÄj. (I. 100), Viá¹£á¹‡u Dh. S. (63. 1), and Laghu-VyÄsa (II. 8) say that a brÄhmaá¹‡a should approach a [[112]] king (or a rich man) for his yogaká¹£ema (i.e. for his livelihood and support). Manu (IV. 33), YÄj. (I. 130), and Vas. Dh. S. (XII. 2) declare that a brÄhmaá¹‡a when oppressed by hunger should seek for help (or money) from a king, from his pupil or from one who is able and willing to offer a sacrifice. But a brÄhmaá¹‡a should not receive a gift from an irreligious king or other irreligious donor. This implies that if the brÄhmaá¹‡a is not hungry and has sufficient wealth either obtained by inheritance or partition or in any other way he should not go about seeking for wealth and should not receive gifts (Manu IV. 34). If a brÄhmaá¹‡a cannot secure gifts from the above three, then he may do so from any other worthy dvijÄti.21 When even that is not possible and the brÄhmaá¹‡a is in difficulties he was allowed to take a gift from anybody including a Å›Å«dra (Manu X. 102-103, YÄj. III. 41); but a brÄhmaá¹‡a should not seek gifts from a Å›Å«dra for the performance of a sacrifice or for agnihotra, as thereby he becomes a cÄá¹‡á¸Äla in another birth (Manu XI. 24 and 42, YÄj. I. 127). A brÄhmaá¹‡a trying to support his hungry elders (parents etc.), his dependants (wife, servants etc.), and about to worship gods and honour guests may accept a gift from anybody (except a patita), but should not satisfy his own hunger with that wealth (Manu IV. 251, Vas. 14. 13, Viá¹£á¹‡u Dh. S. 57. 13, YÄj. I. 216). YÄj., however, allowed this even for one’s own livelihood. Gaut.22 (18. 24-25) allows a brÄhmaá¹‡a to receive even from a Å›Å«dra as much as would enable him to finish marriage ceremonies on which he has embarked or to get materials for a sacrifice when he has begun it. One may take gifts from a Å›Å«dra or ugra for paying a fee to one’s guru at the end of the period of brahmacarya (Ä€p. Dh. S. I. 2. 7. 20-21). A brÄhmaá¹‡a was not to seek gifts from a king who was not of ká¹£atriya lineage nor from butchers, oilmen, keepers of liquor shops and of brothels nor from courtesans (Manu IV. 84), nor from a king who is avaricious and transgresses the rules of the Å›Ästras23 (YÄj. I. 140, Manu IV. 87). The smá¹›tis lay down [[113]] that it is the duty of the king to support Å›rotriyas (brÄhmaá¹‡as learned in the Veda) and brÄhmaá¹‡as who are incapable of struggling for their maintenance (Gaut. X. 9-10, YÄj. III. 44, Atri 24) and that if a Å›rotriya perishes through hunger in the domains of a king, that country would suffer from famine and disease (Manu VII. 134). YÄj. III. 44 lays down that it is the duty of a king to assign a proper means of livelihood to a brÄhmaá¹‡a in distress, having regard to the brÄhmaá¹‡a’s conduct, descent, learning, Vedic study, tapas, the members in his family.24 The ideal set before brÄhmaá¹‡as in the matter of pratigraha (receiving gifts) was that he, who though entitled to accept a gift (on account of his Vedic learning and tapas) does not take it, attains to the highest worlds (YÄj. I. 213); and Manu (IV. 186) says that though entitled to accept gifts a brÄhmaá¹‡a should not again and again resort to that method, since the spiritual power (due to Vedic study) that he acquires is lost by accepting gifts. Another rule about gifts is laid down in many works as follows. When a donor himself goes to the place of a worthy donee and makes a gift that is the best gift, when a donor calls a donee to his place and makes a gift it is middling and when a donor gives if begged by a donee it is inferior.25 Manu (IV. 188-191) prescribes that a man, who is not learned, is reduced to ashes like fuel if he accepts a gift of gold, land, horses, cows, food, clothes, sesame and ghee; that a brÄhmaá¹‡a who, being devoid of learning and tapas (regulated life), desires to accept gifts sinks (into Hell) as one who sits in a stone boat sinks in water; and that therefore a brÄhmaá¹‡a who is not learned should be afraid of receiving gifts.

To the same effect are YÄj. (I. 200-202), Vas. Dh. S. (VI. 32), {{Ausanaga|AuÅ›anasa}} (JÄ«v. I. p. 521 which is almost the same as Manu IV. 188). Just as a brÄhmaá¹‡a who was not learned was enjoined not to accept a gift, so conversely people were asked to make gifts only to learned and worthy men. Even so early as the Åšat. Br. this is emphasized as in IV. 3. 4. 15 (S. B. E. vol. 26, p. 344) “thus those cows of his are given to him who is fit to receive a daká¹£iá¹‡Ä and not to him who is unfit”. Vide III. 5. 1. 19 (S. B. E. vol. 26, p. 114) also. The Ä€p. Dh. S. (II. 6. 15. 9-10) prescribes ‘one should invite for dinner in all religious acts brÄhmaá¹‡as who are pure and who have studied the Veda and one should distribute gifts at a proper time and place and on occasions of purificatory rites and when there is a worthy acceptor’.26 To the same effect are Vas. Dh. S. III. 8 and VI. 30, Manu (III. 128, 132 and IV. 31), YÄj. (I. 201), Daká¹£a III. 26 and 31.27 The smá¹›tis say that gifts given to a brÄhmaá¹‡a who has not studied the Veda or who is avaricious and deceitful are fruitless and lead the donor to hell (Manu IV. 192-194, Atri 152, Daká¹£a III. 29). Manu (XI. 1-3) says that nine kinds of snÄtaka brÄhmaá¹‡as who are poor are the primary objects of the gift of food and fees inside the sacrificial altar; while to others food and wealth may be given outside the altar (bahirvedi). [[114]]

An exception was made in the case of gifts made without request from the donee. What has been offered unasked may be accepted even from one who is guilty of bad actions, except from unchaste women, impotent persons and patita (outcastes or persons guilty of mahÄpÄtakas) YÄj. I. 215, Manu IV. 248-249, Ä€p. Dh. S. I. 6. 19. 11-14 (where two verses are quoted from a PurÄá¹‡a which are almost the same as Manu IV. 248-249), Viá¹£á¹‡u Dh. S. 57. 11. Many persons are mentioned in the smá¹›tis from whom gifts (particularly of food) were not to be accepted (vide Manu IV. 205-224, Vas. Dh. S. XIV. 2-11).

Proper donees

Another rule about gifts was that a person should prefer a learned brÄhmaá¹‡a who is his neighbour for making a gift to one who is not near; if he did not do so, he incurred sin; but there was no blame in passing over an ignorant or foolish [[115]] brÄhmaá¹‡a who stays near in favour of a worthy but distant brÄhmaá¹‡a.28 Vide Vas. Dh. S. III. 9-10, Manu VIII. 392 (which prescribes a fine of one mÄá¹£a for this), Veda-VyÄsa-smá¹›ti IV. 35-38, Bá¹›haspati-smá¹›ti 60 and Laghu-ÅšÄtÄtapa 76-79, Gobhila-smá¹›ti II. 66-69. Devala quoted by AparÄrka p. 288 and Par. M. I. part 1, p. 181 say that that brÄhmaá¹‡a is a pÄtra (worthy to receive a gift) who is pure in three respects (viz. as to his parents and his guru), whose means of livelihood are slender, who is tender-hearted and of restrained senses. Vas. Dh. S. VI. 26 and YÄj. I. 200 also define pÄtra similarly.

It is not to be supposed that the ideal of poverty and non-acceptance of gifts except under compelling circumstances was only an ideal hardly ever acted upon at any time or in any part of the country. Even in the 20th century rural India has villages with a considerable population of brÄhmaá¹‡as where many Å›rotriyas (learned in the Veda) and paá¹‡á¸its (those who study Å›Ästras like grammar, logic, mÄ«mÄá¹ƒsÄ) are still found who are content with what little patrimony they have got, who engage in the profession of teaching the Veda and Å›Ästras in accordance with ancient rules and who do not go about seeking gifts nor accept invitations for dinner at Å›rÄddhas. In the ÅšÄntiparva 199 brÄhmaá¹‡as are divided into two sorts, viz. those who are pravá¹›tta (i.e. engage in all sorts of activities for acquiring wealth) and those who are nivá¹›tta and verse 40 defines these latter as those who do not resort to pratigraha (acceptance of gifts).

Though pratigraha was a special privilege of brÄhmaá¹‡as, gifts could be made by anybody to anybody. YÄj. I. 6 says that giving to a worthy person at a proper time is the complete definition of dharma and ViÅ›varÅ«pa adds that gifts could be made by anybody; but the merit secured by making a gift depended upon the worth and caste of the donee. Gaut. (V. 18),29 Manu VII. 85, Veda-VyÄsa IV. 42, Daká¹£a III, [[116]] 28 say that a gift given to a brÄhmaá¹‡a (who is only so by caste, but is not learned), to a Å›rotriya (or ÄcÄrya) and to one who has completely mastered all the Vedas (with their subsidiary lores) respectively confers merit which is twice, a hundred thousand times or an infinite number of times more than the merit conferred by a gift to a non-brÄhmaá¹‡a. Gautama30 (V. 19-20) and BaudhÄyana II. 3. 24 further make it obligatory to give outside the sacrificial altar according to one’s ability a portion of one’s wealth to a brÄhmaá¹‡a, Å›rotriya and vedapÄraga when they seek help for giving a daká¹£iá¹‡Ä to their teacher (at the end of the period of studenthood), for their own marriage, for medicine, for their own maintenance (that day), for a sacrifice, for their own study or journey, and when everything has been given in a ViÅ›vajit sacrifice and that one must give cooked food to all others who beg for it (except brÄhmaá¹‡a, Å›rotriya and vedapÄraga). Manu (XI. 1-3) gives practically the same rule. In the VaiÅ›vadeva the householder was enjoined to give food to every one including even dogs and cÄá¹‡á¸Älas, as we shall see later on (under31 VaiÅ›vadeva). MedhÄtithi on Manu IV. 5 says that when a person makes a gift through compassion it is not the dÄna and pratigraha spoken of by the Å›Ästra; just as when a man gives instruction as to what is beneficial he does not care to see what the caste of the person to be benefitted by the advice is, so a gift made through compassion is made irrespective of caste; and that when non-brÄhmaá¹‡as reduced to a helpless condition take what is given by others, it cannot be said that they are assuming to themselves the livelihood by pratigraha which is a peculiar means of livelihood for brÄhmaá¹‡as.32

In spite of the noble ideal set before brÄhmaá¹‡as it appears that, owing to the growth of the BrÄhmaá¹‡a population and the paucity of gifts and invitations to officiate as priests, the strict rules about dÄna and pratigraha had to be relaxed and in course of time it came to be said that a brÄhmaá¹‡a, whether learned or ignorant, was to be a donee and may accept gifts without any scruples. The first inroads33 was made by the rule that in rites meant for gods the character and learning of brÄhmaá¹‡as need not be deeply inquired into, but that such enquiry was proper only when they were to be invited for Å›rÄddha and other rites for the dead, the only exceptions being that a brÄhmaá¹‡a, who is a thief or is guilty of a mahÄpÄtaka, or is impotent or an atheist was not to be invited even in rites for the gods (vide Manu III. 149-150). Gradually such views as the following came to be recommended. The SkandapurÄá¹‡a34 as quoted by AparÄrka (p. 455) makes Åšiva say to PÄrvatÄ« ’the Vedic revelation is that Å›rÄddha (food) should be given (to a brÄhmaá¹‡a) after inquiry (into his learning and character), but straightforward action is better than scrutiny. When one offers Å›rÄddha straightforwardly without scrutiny, his pitá¹›s are satisfied and also gods.’ The Vá¹›ddha-Gautama smá¹›ti (chap. III, pp. 512-513 and 518, Jiv.) says ‘BrÄhmaá¹‡as, whether well conducted or of bad conduct, whether vulgar or of polished intellect, should not be disrespected like fires covered with ashes. Just as fire in whatever condition it may be, is a great deity, so a brÄhmaá¹‡a is a great deity in whatever condition he may be’.35 “The wise should not despise brÄhmaá¹‡as, whether they be squint-eyed, humpbacked, dwarfs, indigent or diseased, since they are [[118]] my forms (i.e. of Ká¹›á¹£á¹‡a)”. The AnuÅ›Äsanaparva (152. 19)36 says ‘a brÄhmaá¹‡a who is not learned is a god and he is a worthy object for gifts and is a great purifier; a learned brÄhmaá¹‡a is a greater god (than an unlearned one).’

As already said above teaching could have brought very little wealth. There was no state educational system as in modern times with stability of tenure and graded rates of salary. Nor was there any Copyright Act under which a learned man could make money by writing books for students and the general public. The brÄhmaá¹‡as had no organised corporate body like the Anglican Church with its hierarchy of Archbishops, Bishops and other divines, nor was there in ancient India any practice of making wills whereby large estates came to the Church as in England (where statutes of Mortmain had to be passed to prevent enormous estates from going to the Church).37 The emoluments of officiating priests and gifts given by charitably disposed persons must have been fitful and offered only a precarious means of livelihood, as they depended upon the volition of others and as the smá¹›tis recommended that even in Å›rÄddha too many brÄhmaá¹‡as should not be invited.38 Besides all brÄhmaá¹‡as could not have possessed the memory, intelligence and patience required to master the Vedic Literature after intensive study for decades of years. Therefore, there is no wonder that many brÄhmaá¹‡as were compelled by the force of circumstances to pursue for their livelihood avocations other than the three prescribed ones. From ancient times this was recognised by the dharmaÅ›Ästra works. Gaut. (VII. 6 and 7) says that if a brÄhmaá¹‡a cannot maintain himself by means of the three peculiar modes of livelihood viz. teaching or officiating as a priest for even an unworthy person or by receiving gifts, then he should maintain himself by doing the work peculiar to a ká¹£atriya (i.e. by fighting and protecting people) and if even that is not possible then by following the avocations of a vaiÅ›ya and Gaut. VII. 26 ordains that a ká¹£atriya may resort to the profession of the vaiÅ›ya in similar circumstances. BaudhÄyana [[119]] Dh. S. (II. 2. 77) says the same and then it adds (II. 2. 78 and 80) that Gautama says that it should not be so as the duties of a ká¹£atriya would be too terrific for a brÄhmaá¹‡a and that he should pursue the avocation of a vaiÅ›ya. BaudhÄyana (I. 1. 20) notes that the profession of arms was practised by the brÄhmaá¹‡as of the north. Vas. Dh. S. (II. 22) lays down that persons (of the three higher vará¹‡as) should, if they cannot maintain themselves by the peculiar avocations of their vará¹‡a, resort to the means of livelihood prescribed for the vará¹‡a which is immediately below their own. Manu X. 81-82, YÄj. III. 35, NÄrada (á¹›á¹‡ÄdÄna 56), Viá¹£á¹‡u Dh. S. 54. 28, Åšaá¹…kha-Likhita say the same39 thing. It is further laid down by the same works that a person belonging to a lower vará¹‡a should not resort to the modes of livelihood peculiar to a higher vará¹‡a (vide Vas. Dh. S. II. 23, Manu X. 95). The smá¹›tis further ordain that when the calamity or distress ceases, the person who has taken to the avocations of another vÄrá¹‡a should perform prÄyaÅ›citta, should resume his proper avocations and abandon the wealth acquired by him by resorting to improper avocations; vide Manu XI. 192-193; Viá¹£á¹‡u Dh. S. (54. 27-28), YÄj. III. 35, NÄrada (á¹›á¹‡ÄdÄna 59-60). Manu (X. 96) prescribes that, if a person of a lower vará¹‡a maintains himself through greed by the avocation40 peculiar to a higher vará¹‡a, the king should confiscate the wealth and should at once banish him from the country. A classical example of the keenness with which good kings were expected to prevent persons of lower vará¹‡as doing the actions allowed only to higher vará¹‡as is furnished by the story of ÅšambÅ«ka narrated in the RÄmÄyaá¹‡a (VII. 73-76). The UttararÄmacarita of BhavabhÅ«ti echoes the same sentiments. A Å›Å«dra41 who engaged in japa, homa, tapas or became an ascetic or repeated (Vedic) mantras was to be punished (or killed) by the king and was also guilty of mortal sin. Manu (X. 98) allows a vaiÅ›ya, if unable to maintain himself by the pursuits peculiar to his vará¹‡a, to live by means of the [[120]] actions proper for a Å›Å«dra viz. serving members of the twice-born classes. It is also said by Gaut. (VII. 22-24) that a brÄhmaá¹‡a may maintain himself in any way if unable to maintain himself (by the three means specially prescribed for him), but he should not resort to the actions peculiar to a Å›Å«dra, that according to some ÄcÄryas he may do even those actions when life itself is in danger, but that when he stoops to the actions peculiar to Å›Å«dras for maintenance he should not mix himself up with members of that vará¹‡a (by sitting on the same seat with them etc.) or eat articles forbidden to brÄhmaá¹‡as (such as leek and garlic) and should not be a mere menial servant. Vide Manu (IV. 4 and 6) and NÄrada (á¹›á¹‡ÄdÄna 57).

According42 to all ancient authorities the special duty of the Å›Å«dra was to render service to the twice-born classes, to obtain his livelihood from them and serving a brÄhmaá¹‡a conferred greater happiness or benefit on the Å›Å«dra than serving a ká¹£atriya and serving a ká¹£atriya conferred greater good than serving a vaiÅ›ya. According to Gaut. (X. 60-61), Manu (X. 124-125) and others, the Å›Å«dra was to wear the old or cast-off clothes, umbrellas, sandals, mattress etc. of his patron and the leavings of food (ucchiá¹£á¹­a) were to be given to him. If he became old and unable to do work while serving anyone of the higher vará¹‡as he was to be fed by him whom he had formerly served (Gaut. X. 63). In course of time the position of the Å›Å«dra improved. If a Å›Å«dra43 was unable to maintain himself and his family by serving dvijas, he was allowed to maintain himself by having recourse to crafts like carpentry or drawing or painting pictures etc. NÄrada (á¹›á¹‡ÄdÄna 58) allowed him to perform the work of ká¹£atriyas and vaiÅ›yas in times of distress. YÄj. (I. 120) also says that, if unable to maintain himself by the service of dvijas, the Å›Å«dra may carry on the profession of a vaiÅ›ya or may take to the various crafts. The MahÄbhÄrata allowed a Å›Å«dra44 who could not maintain himself by the service of higher vará¹‡as to resort to the avocations of a vaiÅ›ya, to rearing cattle and to crafts. Others like Laghu-Ä€Å›valÄyana (22. 5), Vá¹›ddha-HÄrÄ«ta (VII. 189, 192) allowed agriculture to the Å›Å«dra. The KÄlikÄpurÄá¹‡a quoted in the Gá¹›. R. (p. 479) allowed the Å›Å«dra to sell all commodities except honey, skins, lÄká¹£Ä (lac), wines and flesh, while Bá¹›hat-ParÄÅ›ara (p. 101) prohibited the Å›Å«dra from selling wine and flesh. Devala quoted in the Mit. (on YÄj. I. 120) prescribes that the Å›Å«dra should serve the twice-born and may engage in agriculture, rearing cattle, carrying loads, sale of commodities, drawing and painting, dancing, singing and playing on musical instruments like the flute, lute, drums and tabors.45 The foregoing will show that the Å›Å«dra gradually rose in social status so far as occupation was concerned and could follow all occupations except those specially reserved for the brÄhmaá¹‡a, so much so that Å›Å«dras became even kings and Manu (IV. 61) had expressly to enjoin upon brÄhmaá¹‡as not to dwell in the kingdom of a Å›Å«dra.46 The smá¹›tis however did not like that wealth should be accumulated in the hands of the Å›Å«dra (though they were quite willing that ká¹£atriyas and vaiÅ›yas should command all wealth). Gaut. (X. 64-65) says that the Å›Å«dra’s accumulation of wealth should be for the support and benefit of the other vará¹‡as. Manu (X. 129) says that a Å›Å«dra, even though able to accumulate wealth, should not do so, as (on account of his pride of wealth and his ignorance) he may cause obstructions and trouble to brÄhmaá¹‡as. ÅšÅ«dras were divided into numerous subcastes. But there were two main divisions. One was aniravasita Å›Å«dras (such as carpenters and blacksmiths) and the other niravasita Å›Å«dras (like cÄá¹‡á¸Älas); vide note 200 above. Another division of Å›Å«dras was into those who were bhojyÄnna (i.e. food prepared by whom could be partaken by brÄhmaá¹‡as) and abhojyÄnna. In the first were included one’s [[122]] slave, one’s cowherd, barber, family friend and one who shared with one the crop reared on one’s land (vide YÄj. I. 166). It is worthy of note that even the Mit. adds the potter to the above list. All the other Å›Å«dras were such that a brÄhmaá¹‡a could not take his food. A third and well-known division was into sacchÅ«dra (well-conducted) and asac-chÅ«dra. The former class included those Å›Å«dras who followed good occupations or trade, served dvijas and had given up meat and drinking or selling liquor.47 The ÅšÅ«drakamalÄkara (p. 60) says that asat-Å›Å«dras do not incur sin even if they partake of meat and liquor, provided they do not eat forbidden meat and that there is no lapse if one comes in contact with a Å›Å«dra that drinks liquor.

A few words may now be said about brÄhmaá¹‡as being allowed to follow the occupations of ká¹£atriyas and vaiÅ›yas. From very ancient times brÄhmaá¹‡as appear to have followed the profession of arms. PÄá¹‡ini (V. 2. 71) teaches48 the formation of the word ‘brÄhmaá¹‡aka’ as applied to a country, which means “in which BrÄhmaá¹‡as follow the profession of arms”. Kauá¹­ilya49 (IX. 2) quotes the view of the ÄcÄryas that when there are armies composed of brÄhmaá¹‡as, ká¹£atriyas, vaiÅ›yas and Å›Å«dras each preceding one is better for enlistment than each subsequent, but Kauá¹­ilya himself is against this and adds that the enemy may win over the army of brÄhmaá¹‡as by prostration before them. Ä€pastamba was against the idea of brÄhmaá¹‡as following the profession of arms. He says (I. 10. 29. 7)50 ‘a brÄhmaá¹‡a should not catch hold of a weapon even for examining it (much [[123]] less for attacking others with it)’. Gaut. (VII. 6) allowed a brÄhmaá¹‡a to follow a ká¹£atriya’s profession in case of distress (Äpad) and adds (in VII. 25) that even a brÄhmaá¹‡a while still following the peculiar avocation of a brÄhmaá¹‡a may wield weapons when his life is in danger. The Baudh. Dh. S. (II. 2. 80) quotes a verse ‘for saving cows and brÄhmaá¹‡as, for preventing the mixture of vará¹‡as, the brÄhmaá¹‡a and vaiÅ›ya may take to arms from their concern for dharma.’ The Vas. Dh. S. (III. 24) allows a brÄhmaá¹‡a to wield a weapon for protecting himself and for preventing confusion or mingling of vará¹‡as. Manu (VIII. 348-349) allows all dvijÄtis to resort to weapons where the observance of dharma (or of the duties of vará¹‡as and ÄÅ›ramas) is obstructed (by violent men), when there is a disturbance (due to invasion etc.) involving the twice-born classes, in evil times for protecting one’s self, when there is an attack for carrying away cows or other wealth (given as fees) and in order to protect women and brÄhmaá¹‡as and he incurs no sin if he kills (for these purposes). Among the heroes of the MahÄbhÄrata there are great warriors and commanders like Droá¹‡a, his son AÅ›vatthÄman, Ká¹›pa (the maternal uncle of AÅ›vatthÄman) who were brÄhmaá¹‡as. The MahÄbhÄrata says that a brÄhmaá¹‡a should fight at the order of the king.51 The ÅšÄntiparva (78. 18) calls upon persons of all vará¹‡as to wield arms when the rules for holding society together are broken and when dasyus (robbers or low persons) cause confusion. From ancient times we find brÄhmaá¹‡as as commanders and founders of royal dynasties. The famous SenÄpati Puá¹£yamitra belonged to the Åšuá¹…ga gotra and wrested an empire from the last of the Mauryas about 184 B.C. His line was followed by the KÄá¹‡vÄyanas, the founder being minister VÄsudeva, a brÄhmaá¹‡a, who killed the last Åšuá¹…ga about 72 B.C. We learn from the Talagunda pillar inscription of KÄkutsthavarman (E. I. Vol. VIII, p. 24) that the founder of the Kadambas, MayÅ«raÅ›arman, was a brÄhmaá¹‡a. In MarÄá¹­ha history there were the Peshwas and other brÄhmaá¹‡a warriors and commanders.

Though it is said that a brÄhmaá¹‡a in distress may follow the occupation of a vaiÅ›ya, there were several restrictions imposed upon brÄhmaá¹‡as following the occupations of money-lending, agriculture, trade, and the rearing of cattle, which were prescribed as the privileged occupations of vaiÅ›yas. [[124]]

As to money-lending, Gaut.52 (X. 5-6) allowed a brÄhmaá¹‡a to maintain himself and his family by agriculture, sale of commodities and money-lending only if he did not engage in these personally, but through the agency of others. Vas. Dh. S. (II. 40) enjoins upon brÄhmaá¹‡as and ká¹£atriyas not to lend money like usurers and quotes two verses which define usury and say that a usurer is a greater sinner than even one who is guilty of brÄhmaá¹‡a-murder. Manu (X. 117) also forbids usury to brÄhmaá¹‡as and ká¹£atriyas, but allows them to charge a low rate of interest to persons engaged in low actions. NÄrada (á¹›á¹‡ÄdÄna 111) forbids usury to brÄhmaá¹‡as even in the direst calamities. Ä€p. Dh. S. (I. 9. 27. 10) prescribes a prÄyaÅ›citta53 for a brÄhmaá¹‡a lending money at usurious rates. Bá¹›haspati as quoted in the Gá¹›. R. has a rather amusing verse54 ‘sages have enumerated numerous means of livelihood, but out of all of them money-lending is pre-eminent. There is loss in agriculture due to draught, to the fear of the exactions of the king and the ravages of rats and others, but there is no such loss in money-lending.’ It appears that this is only a general or satirical statement and does not recommend money-lending to brÄhmaá¹‡as.

The obvious reasons and motives underlying these restrictions on brÄhmaá¹‡as were to make them live simple lives, to insist on the necessity and high value to themselves and to society of studying, preserving and augmenting the ancient literature and culture, to emphasize the fact that a highly spiritual life should not be given up for a mere secular life, to prevent the coarsening and hardening of the heart and emotions in a relentless and continuous pursuit of wealth or martial glory.

Agriculture and brÄhmaá¹‡as

Agricultureâ€”In the dharmaÅ›Ästra works there is a great conflict of views about agriculture as an occupation for [[125]] brÄhmaá¹‡as. The Vedic Literature does not condemn agriculture in the case of brÄhmaá¹‡as. The gambler’s song55 (á¹šg. X. 34) winds up with the exhortation ‘do not play with dice, do engage in agriculture, thinking highly of my words (or of wealth), do find joy in wealth, in that (in agriculture) there are cows, there is your wife &c.’ There are frequent references in Vedic Literature to fields, ploughshares and tilling the soil (vide á¹šg. X. 101. 3 = Tai. S. IV. 2. 5. 5, VÄj. S. XII. 67, á¹šg. I. 110. 5, I. 176. 2, X. 117. 7). BaudhÄyana56 says (I. 5. 101) ‘The study of the Veda tends to the destruction of agriculture and (devotion to) agriculture tends to the loss of the (study) of the Veda. One who has the capacity (to look after both) may resort to both, but he who is unable (to look after both) should give up agriculture’. BaudhÄyana further says (II. 2. 82-83) ‘a brÄhmaá¹‡a should engage in agriculture before his morning meal and he should only coax again and again his oxen whose noses have not been pierced and whose testicles are not removed and without prodding them with a pointed awl’. The Vas. Dh. S. (II. 32-34) has a similar sÅ«tra, adds that in summer he shall water his beasts (in the morning) and quotes VÄj. S. XII. 71. Manu (X. 83-84) says ‘a brÄhmaá¹‡a or a ká¹£atriya compelled to follow the avocations of a vaiÅ›ya (owing to difficulty of maintenance otherwise) should by all means avoid agriculture which is full of injury to sentient beings and dependent on others (labourers, oxen &c.). Some regard agriculture as a good mode of livelihood but it is condemned by the good, (as) wood having an iron tip (i.e. the plough) strikes the earth and (the insects and germs) imbedded in the earth.’ Manu IV. 5 designated agriculture by the word ‘pramá¹›ta’ (pre-eminent in loss of life). HÄrÄ«ta quoted57 in Gá¹›. R. p. 429 declares ’the ploughshare (i.e. agriculture) carries with it destruction of life, therefore it is not for brÄhmaá¹‡as; but if he were to follow it (agriculture) in distress he should pursue it only till his object (of tiding over [[126]] distress) is accomplished’. ParÄÅ›ara58 (II. 2-4, 7, 14) allows a brÄhmaá¹‡a to engage in agriculture, but lays down certain restrictions. ‘The proper number of oxen to be yoked to the plough is eight, six being middling, four are yoked only by the cruel and two by those who sacrifice the lives of their oxen; he should not yoke an ox that is hungry, thirsty or tired, he should make the oxen work only for half the day and then bathe them in water, he should offer the five mahÄyajÃ±as and other sacrifices with corn raised by himself engaging in agriculture; the sin of ploughing the earth for a day with an iron-tipped ploughshare is equal to that incurred by a fisherman fishing for a year; he should give 1/6th of the corn to the king, offer 1/21st to gods and 1/30th to brÄhmaá¹‡as and then he may not be smeared with sin’. HÄrÄ«ta quoted by AparÄrka (p. 937) has a long prose passage on the treatment to be given to oxen by brÄhmaá¹‡as and also Vá¹›ddha-Gautama (Jiv. part 2, p. 571). Vá¹›ddha-HÄrÄ«ta59 (VII. 179 and 182) says that agriculture is common to all vará¹‡as and agriculture, rearing cattle and service are not forbidden to any. The above discussion shows how agriculture was viewed at different times and by different writers from different points of view.

Sale and barter for brÄhmaá¹‡as

Sale and barterâ€”We have seen above that a brÄhmaá¹‡a is allowed to maintain himself by trade in distress or difficulties (Äpad). But there were very great restrictions as to what things could be sold by a brÄhmaá¹‡a. According to Gaut. (VII. 8-14) a brÄhmaá¹‡a should not engage in the sale of fragrant things (like sandal-wood), fluids (like oils, ghee &c.), cooked food, sesame, hemp (and hempen articles like bags), ká¹£auma (linen), deer-skin, dyed and cleanly washed clothes, milk and its products (like curds &c.), roots, flowers, fruits, herbs (used as drugs), honey, meat, grass, water, deleterious drugs (like opium, poison), animals (for being killed), men (as slaves), barren cows, heifers and cows liable to abortion. He adds (Gautama VII. 15) that according to some a brÄhmaá¹‡a could not sell land, rice, yava, goats and sheep, horses, bulls, freshly delivered cows and [[127]] oxen that are yoked to carts. These restrictions did not apply to a ká¹£atriya engaging in trade. Ä€p. Dh. S. I. 7. 20. 12-1360 has a similar list but adds among forbidden articles of sale ‘weapons, sticky things (Å›leá¹£ma, like lac), young stalks (tokma), fermented liquids (kiá¹‡va), the expectation of merit (suká¹›tÄÅ›Ä)’ and says that among corns sesame and rice are on no account to be sold. Baudh. Dh. S. II. 1. 77-78 condemns the sale of sesame and rice by saying that he who sells them sells respectively his pitá¹›s (dead ancestors) and his prÄá¹‡as. This arose probably from the close connection of sesame with Å›rÄddha and tarpaá¹‡a. Vas. Dh. S. (II. 24-29) gives a similar list and adds a prohibition against the sale of stones, salt, silk, iron, tin, lead, all wild animals, all tame animals with uncloven hoofs and those that have a mane, birds and animals having fangs. It quotes a verse at II. 27 (which is the same as Manu X. 92) ‘a brÄhmaá¹‡a immediately becomes a sinner by the sale of meat, lac and salt and he becomes a Å›Å«dra by selling milk for three days’. About sesame, Baudh. Dh. S.61 (II. 1. 76), Manu (X. 91) and Vas. Dh. S. (II. 30) present the same verse. ‘If a man deals with sesame in any way other than eating them or using them for bathing (i.e. applying sesamum oil to the body before a bath) and making a gift of them, he becomes a worm and sinks together with his pitá¹›s in the ordure of a dog’. But it appears that Vasiá¹£á¹­ha62 (II. 31), Manu (X. 90) allow the sale of sesame if a man engages in agriculture and himself produces them (but sale must be only for purposes of religious duties, according to Manu). YÄj. (III. 39) and NÄrada (á¹›á¹‡ÄdÄna 66) say that sesame may be bartered for an equal measure of other corn to raise means for religious purposes and for medicine also (according to NÄrada). Manu (X. 86-89), YÄj. (III. 36-38), NÄrada (á¹›á¹‡ÄdÄna 61-63) give long lists of articles that BrÄhmaá¹‡as were forbidden to sell and that include a few more than those specified above. For example, Manu forbids the sale of bee’s wax, kuÅ›a, indigo, while YÄj. adds soma, mud, blankets made of goat wool, hair (of camarÄ« deer) and oilcakes (piá¹‡yÄka) to things forbidden to [[128]] be sold. Åšaá¹…kha-Likhita,63 Udyoga-parva 38. 5, ÅšÄntiparva 78. 4-6, HÄrÄ«ta (as quoted by AparÄrka p. 1113) contain long lists of things the sale of which was forbidden to brÄhmaá¹‡as. Apart from these negative rules, there are some that are positive and prescribe what may be sold. For example, the Baudh. Dh. S.64 prescribes the sale of grass and wood in their natural state and quotes a verse ‘Oh! BrÄhmaá¹‡a, these are the articles you may sell, viz., domestic animals that have only one row of teeth, minerals except salt and threads (i.e. cloth) that are not coloured with some dye.’ NÄrada65 (á¹›á¹‡ÄdÄna 64-65) states ‘a brÄhmaá¹‡a may sell dried wood and grass, except fragrant articles, eraka (a kind of grass), rattan, cotton, roots, kuÅ›a grass; cereals that get split up of their own accord, badara and iá¹…guda among fruits, cords and threads of cotton provided they are not coloured’. Åšaá¹…kha-Likhita also (as quoted by AparÄrka p. 933) have the same rules as NÄrada and further enjoin upon the brÄhmaá¹‡a not to higgle for the price but to have a fixed price.

YÄj. (III. 40) says that the sale of lac, salt and meat lead to a brÄhmaá¹‡a’s fall (i.e. he loses the right to perform the duties of dvijÄtis) and the sale of milk, curds and liquor reduces him to the status of a low class (i.e. of a Å›Å«dra). Manu (XI. 62), Viá¹£á¹‡u Dh. S. (37. 14) and YÄj. (III. 234) include the sale of forbidden articles among upapÄtakas and YÄj. (III. 265) prescribes cÄndrÄyaá¹‡a and other prÄyaÅ›cittas for it. HÄrÄ«ta (quoted by AparÄrka p. 1113 and Mit. on YÄj. III. 265) prescribes various prÄyaÅ›cittas for the sale of various forbidden articles. Laghu-ÅšÄtÄtapa prescribes (v. 87) cÄndrÄyaá¹‡a for the sale of honey, meat, wine, soma, lac, salt.66 NÄrada (á¹›á¹‡ÄdÄna 67) calls upon [[129]] the king to inflict a heavy fine upon a brÄhmaá¹‡a who engages in the sale of articles forbidden to be sold and strays from the path (proper for brÄhmaá¹‡as) in the absence of distress.

Ä€p. Dh. S. (I. 7. 20. 14-15)67 states the general rule that exchange or barter also of those articles that are forbidden to be sold cannot be resorted to, but adds that barter is allowed of foods with foods, of slaves with slaves, of fragrant things with other fragrant things, of one kind of learning with another. Gaut.68 (VII. 16-21) allows the exchange of rasas with rasas, of domestic animals with other domestic animals, of cooked food with an equal measure of uncooked food for immediate use, but forbids the barter of salt, cooked food and sesame with other articles. Manu (X. 94) allows the exchange of one rasa (liquid like molasses) with another (like ghee), of cooked food with uncooked food, of sesame with an equal quantity of other corn, but does not allow the barter of salt for any rasa. Vas. Dh. S. (II. 37-39) has rules similar to Manu and Ä€p.69

Manu (X. 116)70 enumerates ten means of maintaining oneself in Äpad (distress) viz. learning, arts and crafts, work for wages, service (i.e. carrying out another’s orders), rearing cattle, sale of commodities, agriculture, contentment, alms, money-lending. Out of these some cannot be followed by a brÄhmaá¹‡a or a ká¹£atriya when there is no distress (e.g. a brÄhmaá¹‡a cannot engage in service). YÄj. ChÄgaleya quoted in Gá¹›.
