NÄrada (á¹›á¹‡ÄdÄna 50-55) says that three modes of acquiring wealth are common to all, viz. inheritance, a gift of friendliness or affection and what comes to a man with a wife (at the time of marriage); that each of the three vará¹‡as has three special modes of acquiring wealth, viz., receiving gifts, fees as priest and fees for teaching in the case of brÄhmaá¹‡as; booty in war, taxes and fines in judicial trials in the case of ká¹£atriyas; agriculture, rearing cattle and sale of commodities in the case of vaiÅ›yas. NÄrada (á¹›á¹‡ÄdÄna verses 44-47) divides wealth into Å›ukla (white, pure), Å›abala (dark-white, mixed) and ká¹›á¹£á¹‡a (dark) and each of these into seven varieties. The Viá¹£á¹‡u Dh. S. chap. 58 also divides the wealth of householders into these three varieties and says that what is earned by the special modes prescribed for each vará¹‡a, inherited wealth, gifts of affection and what comes with the wifeâ€”these are Å›ukla (pure); what is obtained by following the special avocation of the vará¹‡a immediately lower than one’s own vará¹‡a and what is acquired by giving bribes or by sale of forbidden articles or from one who is under one’s obligations is Å›abala; what is obtained by following the avocations of a vará¹‡a other than the immediately lower one and what is acquired by gambling, theft, violence or fraud is called ká¹›á¹£á¹‡a. Baudh. Dh. S. (III. 1. 5-6) speaks of ten kinds of vá¹›ttis (means of livelihood) and III. 2 explains them at length. Manu (IV. 4-6) speaks of five ways of livelihood viz. á¹›ta (i.e. subsisting on grains left in the fields), amá¹›ta (what comes without begging), má¹›ta (alms obtained by begging), pramá¹›ta (agriculture), satyÄná¹›ta (sale of commodities) and forbids Å›vavá¹›tti (service, lit. living like a dog). Manu (IV. 9) further says that some brÄhmaá¹‡as live by six means (i.e. adhyÄpana, yÄjana, pratigraha, agriculture, rearing cattle and trade), some by three (viz. the first three), some by two (yÄjana and adhyÄpana) and others again only by one (adhyÄpana).

The avocations practised by brÄhmaá¹‡as in the pursuit of wealth were many and varied, so much so that from very ancient times the lists of brÄhmaá¹‡as not eligible for invitation at Å›rÄddhas because they followed unworthy callings were very formidable. Atri (Anan. ed.) verses 379-383 names ten kinds of brÄhmaá¹‡as and briefly defines them, viz. deva-brÄhmaá¹‡a (who [[131]] daily performs bath, sandhyÄ, japa, homa, worship of gods and honouring of guests and vaiÅ›vadeva), muni-br. (who is given up to staying in a forest, subsists on roots, fruits and vegetables and performs daily Å›rÄddhas), dvija-br. (who studies the VedÄnta, gives up all attachments and is engaged in reflecting over SÄá¹ƒkhya and Yoga), ká¹£atra-br. (who fights), vaiÅ›ya-br. (who engages in agriculture, rearing cattle and trade), Å›Å«dra-br. (who sells lac, salt, dyes like kusumbha, milk, ghee, honey, meat), niá¹£Äda-br. (who is a thief and robber, a backbiter and always fond of fish and meat), paÅ›u-br. (who knows nothing about brahma and is only proud of his wearing the sacred thread), mleccha-br. (who obstructs or destroys wells, tanks, gardens, without any qualm) and caá¹‡á¸Äla-br. (who is a fool, devoid of prescribed rites, beyond the pale of all dharma and cruel). Atri (384) rather72 humorously adds ’those who are devoid of Vedic lore, study the Å›Ästras (like grammar, logic &c.), those devoid of Å›Ästric lore study purÄá¹‡as (and earn money by reciting them), those who are devoid even of purÄá¹‡a reading become agriculturists, those who are devoid even of that become bhÄgavatas (pose as great devotees of Åšiva or Viá¹£á¹‡u i.e. become what is called in modern Marathi ‘buvÄ’).’ Devala quoted73 by AparÄrka (pp. 284-285) speaks of eight kinds of brÄhmaá¹‡as (of whom each succeeding one is superior to each preceding one) viz. mÄtra (one who is only born in a brÄhmaá¹‡a family but has not studied any part of the Veda nor performs the actions appropriate to brÄhmaá¹‡as), brÄhmaá¹‡a (who has studied a portion of the Veda), Å›rotriya (who has studied one vedic Å›ÄkhÄ with the six aá¹…gas and performs the six duties of brÄhmaá¹‡as), anÅ«cÄna (who knows the meaning of the Veda and the vedÄá¹…gas, is of pure heart and has kindled the sacred fires), bhrÅ«á¹‡a (who besides being an anÅ«cÄna always performs yajÃ±as and eats what is left after performing yajÃ±as), á¹›á¹£ikalpa (who has gained all worldly and Vedic knowledge, and has his mind [[132]] under control), á¹›á¹£i (one who is celibate, of austere life, of truthful speech and able to curse or favour), muni (to whom a clod of earth and gold are the same, who has ceased from all activity, is devoid of desires and anger &c.). ÅšÄtÄtapa quoted by AparÄrka (pp. 286-287) speaks of six classes of persons who, though born brÄhmaá¹‡as, are really not brÄhmaá¹‡as viz. one who has taken service with a king, one who engages in sale and purchase (of commodities), one who officiates for many yajamÄnas, one who is the officiating priest for the whole village, one who is in the service of a village or town, one who does not perform sandhyÄ adoration in the morning and evening at the proper time. The AnuÅ›Äsanaparva (33. 11 ff) shows that some brÄhmaá¹‡as were great rogues, others engaged in austerities, some resorted to agriculture and rearing cattle, others subsisted by begging, some were thieves and others were false, some were acrobats and dancers (but it yet recommends that brÄhmaá¹‡as must be honoured).

The smá¹›tis teach that brÄhmaá¹‡as doing certain things are to be treated as Å›Å«dras. For example, Baudh.74 Dh. S. (II. 4. 20) requires a religious king to employ brÄhmaá¹‡as who do not perform the morning and evening adoration (sandhyÄ) in doing work appropriate to Å›Å«dras. Vas.75 Dh. S. (III. 1-2) says that brÄhmaá¹‡as who are not Å›rotriyas (learned in the Veda), who do not teach the Veda or who do not kindle the sacred fires become reduced to the status of Å›Å«dras and quotes a MÄnava Å›loka (Manu II. 168) ’that a brÄhmaá¹‡a who without studying the Veda works hard to master something else is quickly reduced while still living to the status of a Å›Å«dra together with his family.’ Manu (VIII. 102 = Baudh. Dh. S. I. 5. 95) asks the king to treat as Å›Å«dras brÄhmaá¹‡as who engage in rearing cattle, in the sale of commodities, who are artisans and actors, who are mere servants and money-lenders. Vide Manu X. 92 quoted above (p. 127). ParÄÅ›ara (VIII. 24) says76 that a brÄhmaá¹‡a who does not repeat the GÄyatrÄ« mantra is more impure than even a Å›Å«dra and that brÄhmaá¹‡as who do not offer oblations to sacred fires, who are bereft of saá¹ƒdhyÄ adoration and who do not study the Veda are all Å›Å«dras and that therefore one should study at least a portion of the Veda if he cannot study the whole. Manu (V. 4)77 sums up in one place the reasons why brÄhmaá¹‡as are seized by Death before the allotted span of human life ‘on account of not studying the Vedas, on account of giving up the rules of conduct prescribed for them, through idleness and through the faults arising from (partaking forbidden) food, Death desires to kill brÄhmaá¹‡as.’ [[133]]

A few words must be said about begging. The smá¹›tis prescribe begging as specially appropriate to brahmacÄrins (vedic students) and ascetics (yatis), which will be dealt with at length later on. Begging was not allowed to others except under considerable restrictions. The king of Kekaya78 is made to boast in the MahÄbhÄrata (ÅšÄnti 77. 22) that no one who is not a brahmacÄrin begs in his kingdom. Gifts of food were to be made daily while performing the five mahÄyajÃ±as (this will be treated under vaiÅ›vadeva). Ä€p. Dh. S. II. 5. 10. 1-4 recognizes that begging can properly be resorted to for the following reasons, viz. for the teacher, for (one’s first) marriage, for a sacrifice, in anxiety to support one’s parents, for warding off the non-observance of the duties of a worthy person; he enjoins that on these occasions there is a duty to give according to one’s ability and according to the worth of the person begging and that if a man begs only for the gratification of his senses (and not for pressing wants) one should pay no heed to such [[134]] begging. Vas. Dh. S. XII. 2-3 says ‘a man oppressed by hunger may beg for a little viz. a cultivated or uncultivated plot, a cow, a sheep or ewe, and at last gold, corn or cooked food; but a snÄtaka should not faint through hunger; this is the instruction’. Vide Manu X. 114 for practically the same words and Viá¹£á¹‡u Dh. S. 3. 79-80. Baudh. Dh. S. II. 1. 64 includes begging by one who has finished his course of studenthood among actions that make a man impure (aÅ›ucikara). Manu XI. 16-17 says that when a person has had no food for three days he may take away (by theft even) from one who is lower than himself in class as much corn as will be enough for one day, either from the threshing floor or from the field or from his house or from whatever place he can get it, but he should announce his action when the owner asks. Gaut. (18. 28-30) and YÄj. III. 43 are to the same effect. Aá¹…giras79 quoted in Gá¹›. R. (p. 450) allows begging to the diseased, the indigent, to one who is torn away from his family and who is on a journey. Åšaá¹…kha-Likhita80 as quoted in Gá¹›. R. (p. 457) say ‘when a man begs he should state the purpose (such as marriage, completion of sacrifice) of his begging; he should not beg of a woman or of those who are minors or unable to conduct their affairs, nor when the donor is not in a proper place or at a proper time. He should apply the alms to the purpose for which he begged. He should give that portion of the alms which remains unused to priests or any other excellent person’. Vas. Dh. S. (III. 4) and ParÄÅ›ara81 (I. 60) call upon the king to fine that village where persons of the higher classes wander about begging though they are not observers of vows (like brahmacÄrins) and are not studying the Veda. The foregoing will show that indiscriminate begging was never allowed or encouraged by the smá¹›tis even for brÄhmaá¹‡as, much less for others.

It has been seen above how even during the Vedic period brÄhmaá¹‡as had come to be highly eulogised as if they were gods and held superior merely on account of birth. The [[135]] Tai. Br. III. 7. 3 says ‘One should sacrifice in the right hand of a brÄhmaá¹‡a; the brÄhmaá¹‡a indeed is Agni-vaiÅ›vÄnara’. Vide ÅšÄntiparva 343, 13-14, Manu IV. 117, Likhita 31, Vas. Dh. S. 30. 2-5. The same ideas of the sacredness and the superiority of brÄhmaá¹‡as were carried forward and further emphasized by the dharma-Å›Ästra works. Most exaggerated and hyperbolical descriptions of the greatness of brÄhmaá¹‡as are sown broadcast over all the smá¹›tis and the purÄá¹‡as. It is not possible to set out even a small fraction of them. But a few passages may usefully be cited here by way of samples. The Viá¹£á¹‡u Dh. S.82 (19. 20-22) says ’the gods are invisible deities, but brÄhmaá¹‡as are visible deities; the worlds are supported by brÄhmaá¹‡as; the gods stay in heaven by the favour of brÄhmaá¹‡as; words spoken by brÄhmaá¹‡as never come to be untrue’. Manu (I. 100) declares ‘whatever83 wealth exists on this earthâ€”all that belongs to the brÄhmaá¹‡a; the brÄhmaá¹‡a deserves everything on account of his superiority due to his descent (from the mouth of the Creator).’ Manu IX. 313-321 contain a hyperbolical eulogy of the power of brÄhmaá¹‡as, two of which may be set out ‘who would prosper if he oppresses brÄhmaá¹‡as that, when angered, might create other worlds and other guardians of the worlds and that might deprive the deities of their position as deities’ (315); ‘a brÄhmaá¹‡a, whether learned or not, is a great deity’ (verse 317). Manu XI. 84 is ‘a brÄhmaá¹‡a by the very fact of his birth is an object of honour even to the deities.’ ParÄÅ›ara (VI. 52-53) holds ‘whatever defects there may be in vratas, in austerities, or in sacrificial rites, they all vanish when brÄhmaá¹‡as support them. Whatever words are spoken by brÄhmaá¹‡as are spoken by the gods; brÄhmaá¹‡as have all the gods in them and therefore their words do not fail.’84 The MahÄbhÄrata indulges in very [[136]] frequent eulogies of brÄhmaá¹‡as. Ä€diparva (28. 3-4) says85 ‘a brÄhmaá¹‡a, when provoked, becomes fire, the sun, poison, and weapon; a brÄhmaá¹‡a is declared to be the guru of all beings.’ Vanaparva86 (303. 16) says ‘a brÄhmaá¹‡a is the highest light, he is the highest tapas; the sun shines in heaven on account of the salutations made by the brÄhmaá¹‡as’. This and similar dicta closely follow what was expressed in the Vedic period long before e.g. ’the sun would not rise if the brÄhmaá¹‡a did not make sacrifice’ (Åšatapatha II. 3. 1. 5); the refrain of á¹šg. II. 15. 2-9 is that Indra performed his great and heroic works under the intoxication of the soma (offered by the priests in sacrifices). á¹šg. IV. 50. 7-9 say that a king and others for whom the purohita offers prayers win battles, secure booty and help from gods. AnuÅ›Äsana-parva87 (33. 17) says ’they may make a non-deity into a deity and a deity into a non-deity; that man may become a king whom they desire to be so, and he who is not desired by them may be defeated.’ ÅšÄnti (56. 22) declares ‘In this world brÄhmaá¹‡a is the highest being’.

It should not be supposed that the brÄhmaá¹‡as inserted these eulogies solely for the purpose of increasing their importance, and tightening their hold on the other classes. If the other classes had not themselves more or less shared these ideas, no amount of iteration on the part of brÄhmaá¹‡as would have given them the influence which they as a matter of fact wielded. Their influence was a growth of centuries and they themselves were as much parts of the huge edifice of the caste system as the other vará¹‡as. Besides the brÄhmaá¹‡as had no military force behind them. They could only succeed in influencing the other vará¹‡as by persuasion and their own worth. The brÄhmaá¹‡as were the creators and custodians of the vast literature that had grown up, they were the guardians of the culture of ages, they were expected to shoulder the burdens of teaching and preserving the vast literature on such gifts as were voluntarily made. Though many among them did not [[137]] live up to the high ideals set up for their order, there must have been not a few who made as near an approach to the fulfilment of the ideal as possible. It was the greatness of these latter that led to the glorification of the whole order to which they belonged. Learning and tapas are more or less elusive and impalpable, while birth from brÄhmaá¹‡a parents was quite an apparent and palpable thing. Therefore that was seized upon by some writers as the principal reason for the respect to be given to brÄhmaá¹‡as. For centuries human societies have everywhere acquiesced in the government and control exercised by small coteries of the elite, generally the elite of birth, who have guided the destinies of their societies on traditional lines of religious and social order. It must be remembered that the smá¹›ti works also extol the office of the king (which was generally hereditary and is so even now) to the skies. Manu (VII. 4-11) propounds the theory that the king has in him the parts of such gods as Indra and that a king is a great deity in human form.88 The theory of vará¹‡as as conceived by Manu and other smá¹›tikÄras was based upon the idea of the division of labour, on the idea of balancing the rival claims of various sections of the community; it laid greatest emphasis upon the duties of the vará¹‡as rather than upon their rights and privileges. It raised the brÄhmaá¹‡a to the highest pinnacle of reverence, but at the same time it placed before him the ideal of not hankering after temporal power, of leading a life of comparative poverty and of making his knowledge available to the other classes for a scanty and precarious return. It made the military caste feel that they were not all-in-all, but had to look up to some other class as superior to themselves. European writers severely condemn brÄhmaá¹‡as for their greed, selfishness and constant praises of gifts to themselves. But they forget to take account of the circumstances under which the brÄhmaá¹‡as were forced to sing the praises of gifts to them. The ideal before brÄhmaá¹‡as was to lead a life of comparative poverty; they were forbidden to follow many worldly pursuits and depended on the generosity of their patrons. They did not live in monasteries as Buddhist and Christian monks did nor were they entitled to fixed and fat salaries like the bishops in some Christian countries. They had further to bring up their own families and pupils and had themselves also to make gifts to others. In modern capitalist societies those who have brains [[138]] and knowledge tend to become financiers and capitalists, all wealth is {{ountred|centred}} in their hands, they reduce most other men to mere wage-earners who are often hardly better than helots. While finding fault with the brÄhmaá¹‡a writers of over two thousand years ago for the eulogies they bestowed upon themselves, one should not forget that even in the 20th century when the pursuit of scientific studies is professed to have reached its zenith, we hear ecstatic and arrogant eulogies of the white man’s burden, of the great and glorious achievements of the Nordic race and the greater and more glorious future it is destined to attain (vide the very first sentence in Spengler’s ‘The Decline of the West’, English translation by C. F. Atkinson). The brÄhmaá¹‡as never arrogated to themselves the authority to depose kings and to hand over vast territories for ever to whomsoever they liked, as Pope Alexander VI by his Bull of 1493 made over the New World to Isabella of Castille and Ferdinand of Aragon (vide Dean Inge’s ‘Christian Ethics’ 1930, p. 160, where this astounding Bull is set out).

It has been seen already that brÄhmaá¹‡as had the special privileges of teaching, officiating as priests and accepting gifts made as a religious duty. It is desirable that a comprehensive list of all the privileges claimed by brÄhmaá¹‡as (though not always conceded as the sequel will show) should be set out once for all.

BrÄhmaá¹‡a, the guru of all

(1) The brÄhmaá¹‡a was to be guru (object of reverence)89 to all vará¹‡as by the mere fact of birth. Ä€p. Dh. S. (I. 1. 1. 5) says so. Vas. Dh. S. (IV. 1) says ’the system of four vará¹‡as is distinguished by its origin and by the special features of the sacraments (each of them undergoes)’ and quotes á¹šg. X. 90. 12 in support. Manu (I. 31 and 94) says that the Creator produced the four vará¹‡as respectively from his mouth, arms, thighs and feet, that (I. 93) the brÄhmaá¹‡a is by right the master of this whole world on account of his birth from the best limb (i.e. the mouth) of the Creator, on account of his priority (in birth to the ká¹£atriya and others) and on account of possessing Vedic lore. Manu (X. 3) uses almost the same words as Vasiá¹£á¹­ha ’the brÄhmaá¹‡a is the master of the vará¹‡as on account of the peculiar excellence [[139]] (of his caste), on account of his superiority of origin (from the mouth of the Creator), on account of his submitting himself to discipline (or holding up Vedic lore) and on account of the eminence of the sacraments (saá¹ƒskÄras) in his case’. BhÄ«á¹£maparva (121. 35) says that the brÄhmaá¹‡a is superior among all castes. This sense of superiority was carried so far that Ä€pastamba90 (I. 4. 14. 23), Manu (II. 135) and Viá¹£á¹‡u Dh. S. (32. 17) say that a brÄhmaá¹‡a only ten years old was to be honoured as a father by a ká¹£atriya 100 years old.

(2) The brÄhmaá¹‡a was to expound the duties of all other classes, to indicate to them proper conduct and their means of livelihood and they were to abide by his directions and the king was to rule in accordance with such directions (Vas. Dh. S. I. 39-41, Manu VII. 37, X. 2). This is supported by the words of the KÄá¹­hakasaá¹ƒhitÄ91 (IX. 16) and the Tai. Br. that the brÄhmaá¹‡a is indeed the supervisor over the people. The Ait. Br. (37. 5) declares that where the might of ká¹£atriyas is under the control (or direction) of brÄhmaá¹‡as that kingdom becomes prosperous, that kingdom is full of heroes92 &c. This idea is somewhat similar to the teaching of Plato who held that philosophers that had undergone a special training were to rule and were to be politicians, that the government of the best (aristocracy) was the ideal system. The problem is how to find out the best. Ancient India solved it by leaving the decision of knotty points to the learned brÄhmaá¹‡as and the execution of the decisions arrived at by them to the king and the ká¹£atriyas.

(3) ‘The king was the ruler of all, except brÄhmaá¹‡as’ Gautama93 XI. 1. The Mit. on YÄj. II. 4 explains that these words were only laudatory of the greatness of brÄhmaá¹‡as and were not to be taken literally and that the king could punish brÄhmaá¹‡as in appropriate cases. These words of Gautama are a mere echo of certain passages like those in the VÄj. S. (IX. 40) and the Åšat.94 Br. (V. 4. 2. 3 and IX. 4. 3. 16), where it is said [[140]] ‘Soma is the king of us brÄhmaá¹‡as’. The idea was that brÄhmaá¹‡as had to prepare soma and offer it in sacrifices to the gods i.e. they held all wealth for Soma and owed all allegiance to Soma, while other people owed allegiance only to the king. The brÄhmaá¹‡as alone were to eat the sacrificial food and drink Soma (and ká¹£atriyas were to drink only a substitute for Soma). Vide Ait. Br. (chap. 35, khaá¹‡á¸a 4). In the MahÄbhÄrata, however, many kings are described as Somapas. So the practice of not allowing the ká¹£atriyas to drink soma was not universally accepted. Further it was probably not a privilege at all, but only indicates that Soma sacrifices had been mostly neglected by all except brÄhmaá¹‡as.

BrÄhmaá¹‡a and corporal punishment

(4) “The king should exonerate (the brÄhmaá¹‡a) in the six matters, viz. (a brÄhmaá¹‡a) should not be beaten (whipped), he should not have fetters put on him, he should not be mulcted in fines of money, he should not be driven out (of the village or country), he should not be censured, he should not be abandoned” Gaut. VIII. 12-13.95 The Mit. on YÄj. II. 4 explains that these words are not applicable to every brÄhmaá¹‡a, but only to deeply learned brÄhmaá¹‡as described in the preceding sÅ«tras (Gaut. VIII. 4-11). HÄradatta adds that even a learned brÄhmaá¹‡a is treated in this way only if he commits an offence without pre-meditation but only through ignorance or oversight. As to corporal punishment for brÄhmaá¹‡a offenders, the matter stands thus. Gaut. XII. 43 says that there is no corporal punishment for a brÄhmaá¹‡a (even when he being a thief comes to the king confessing his guilt and asking the king to beat him with a heavy club on the head). Vide Manu XI. 99-100 also. Baudh. Dh. S. (I. 10. 18-19) first lays down that a brÄhmaá¹‡a is to undergo no beating for any offence but allows for a brÄhmaá¹‡a guilty of the mortal sins (of brÄhmaá¹‡a-murder, incest, drinking liquor, theft of gold) the punishment of branding on the forehead with red-hot iron and banishment from the country. Manu IX. 237 (=MatsyapurÄá¹‡a 227. 163-164), Viá¹£á¹‡u Dh. S. V. 4-7 prescribe the [[141]] various figures that were to be branded on the forehead of a brÄhmaá¹‡a in the case of various offences. Manu (VIII. 379-380) prescribes shaving of the head for brÄhmaá¹‡a offenders where others would have had to suffer the extreme penalty of death and adds that a brÄhmaá¹‡a was not to be sentenced to death whatever offence he might have committed and that he is to be banished from the country taking with him all his possessions. Even on this the Mit. (on YÄj. II. 81) observes that it applies only when it is the brÄhmaá¹‡a’s first offence (i.e. he may be fined for repeating an offence). YÄj. (II. 270), NÄrada (sÄhasa, verse 10) and Åšaá¹…kha96 prescribe branding and banishment as punishment for brÄhmaá¹‡as (particularly in the case of theft). The brÄhmaá¹‡a was never above being fined. Manu (VIII. 123) prescribes fine and banishment for a perjured brÄhmaá¹‡a witness, while Manu VIII. 378 prescribes heavy fines for a brÄhmaá¹‡a guilty of rape or adultery. The Mit. on YÄj. II. 30297 quotes a verse of Manu (not found in the extant Manusmá¹›ti) that in the case of the brÄhmaá¹‡a corporal punishment takes the form of complete shaving of the head, he is liable to banishment from the town, he may be branded on the forehead and he may be made to ride through the town on the back of an ass (looked upon at all times as the highest form of indignity). Kauá¹­ilya also98 (IV. 8) forbids corporal punishment for brÄhmaá¹‡as in all offences but prescribes branding on the forehead in the same way as Manu (IX. 237) and also banishment and labour on mines. But Kauá¹­ilya makes an exception, viz. he allows a brÄhmaá¹‡a to be drowned in water if he is guilty of high treason, or of forcible entry in the king’s harem or of inciting the enemies of the king &c. KÄtyÄyana99 (quoted by ViÅ›varÅ«pa on YÄj. II. 281) prescribes death sentence even for a brÄhmaá¹‡a when the latter is guilty of the destruction of a foetus, of theft, of [[142]] striking a brÄhmaá¹‡a woman with a weapon and of killing an innocent woman. The Mit. on YÄj. III. 257 thinks it possible that kings sentenced brÄhmaá¹‡as to death in spite of the prohibition of corporal punishment and we have a classical illustration in the death sentence passed on the brÄhmaá¹‡a CÄrudatta by king PÄlaka in the Má¹›cchakaá¹­ika (IX).

The brÄhmaá¹‡a and privileges in courts

The foregoing discussion shows that the only special privilege claimed for the brÄhmaá¹‡a in the law courts of the land was freedom from death sentence or other corporal punishment like whipping, though rarely he was liable to be sentenced to death also. He was subject to the indignities of branding and being paraded on the back of an ass, to fines and banishment. These claims were very moderate as compared with the absurd lengths to which the doctrine of ‘benefit of clergy’ was carried in England and other western countries.100 The clergyman in England was not by birth, he had to be ordained. Yet clergymen claimed that an ordained clerk, a monk or nun charged with serious offences called felonies could be tried only by an ecclesiastical court and this was conceded by the ordinary courts; this privilege was gradually extended to persons not ordained viz. to doorkeepers, readers, exorcists (all of whom merely assisted the clergy) and finally to all who could read or pretended to read a few words from the Bible. Holdsworth, Pollock and Maitland admit that the procedure in the ecclesiastical courts was little better than a farce (P and M vol. I. p. 426, Holdsworth vol. III, p. 296). The ecclesiastic courts never pronounced a judgment of blood, the bishop only deprived the offending clerk of orders, and relegated him to a monastery, or kept him in prison for life or a shorter period and very rarely whipping and branding were ordered. Even in the first quarter of the 20th century, European British subjects and Europeans and Americans in general could claim in British India some startling privileges when charged with criminal offences which even the brÄhmaá¹‡as of over two thousand years ago did not claim. For example, under Sec. 443 of the Criminal Procedure Code of India (as it existed before 1923) they could not be tried by any Indian Magistrate (however senior and experienced) and that in serious cases like murder, even a Sessions Judge who was himself an European British [[143]] subject could not sentence an European British subject to more than one year’s imprisonment (Section 449). Any European or American could claim to be tried by a mixed jury of which not less than one half had to be Europeans or Americans, while an Indian offender could not claim the privilege in his own country that not less than one half of the jury that tried him must be Indians. In England even now a peer indicted for treason or felony must be tried by his peers and not by the tribunals that try ordinary men (vide Halsbury’s Laws of England, 2nd Ed. vol. 25, p. 46). The brÄhmaá¹‡as had to submit to trial in the ordinary courts and the smá¹›tis do not generally provide for trial of brÄhmaá¹‡as in special ecclesiastical courts of their own class. The only approach to this western doctrine of benefit of clergy is to be found in the Ä€p. Dh. S.,101 where it is said that the teacher and others who have authority over a brÄhmaá¹‡a guilty of transgressions should prescribe prÄyaÅ›cittas for him: if he does not abide by their orders, he should be taken to the king who should hand him over to his own purohita; the latter was to prescribe prÄyaÅ›cittas (penances for atonement) for brÄhmaá¹‡a offenders. If the latter did not carry out the penances then he was to break them by disciplines according to their ability except that he (the purohita) was not to prescribe corporal punishment and slavery for brÄhmaá¹‡a offenders.

Taxes and brÄhmaá¹‡as

(5) Most smá¹›tis lay down that a Å›rotriya (a brÄhmaá¹‡a learned in the Veda) was to be free from taxes. Certain passages of the Åšat. Br. suggest that even in those times brÄhmaá¹‡as were not taxed.102 Ä€p. Dh. S.103 II. 10. 26. 10, Vas. Dh. S. 19. 23, Manu VII. 133 say this. Some claimed this exemption for all brÄhmaá¹‡as e.g. Vas. Dh. S. (I. 42-43) says “a king ruling over his subjects according to the rules of the ÅšÄstra should take the sixth part of all wealth except from brÄhmaá¹‡as”; the Viá¹£á¹‡u Dh. S. III. 26 says ‘a king should [[144]] not recover taxes from brÄhmaá¹‡as’. Kauá¹­ilya104 (II. 1) requires the king to make gifts of brahmadeya lands to á¹›tviks (who officiate at solemn sacrifices), teachers, purohitas, Å›rotriyas, that will yield substantial produce and on which no fines and taxes will be levied. The reason assigned was the belief that the king shared in the religious merit accumulated by the brÄhmaá¹‡as. Vas.105 (I. 44-46) explains ‘(freedom from taxation is there) because he (the king) secures the sixth part of the iá¹£á¹­ÄpÅ«rta (the merit due to sacrifices and performing charitable works of public utility) and it is declared (in a BrÄhmaá¹‡a text) that the brÄhmaá¹‡a enriches the Veda, he relieves (others) from calamities and therefore the brÄhmaá¹‡a is not a source of subsistence (i.e. should not be taxed by the king) since Soma is his king; and it is further declared that bliss awaits after death (the king who does not tax brÄhmaá¹‡as).’ The Viá¹£á¹‡u Dh. S. III. 27 says ’they render unto him the tax of religious merit’. Manu VII. 136 says ‘by the religious merit which the Å›rotriya accumulates every day when protected by the king, the king’s life, wealth and kingdom increase.’ Vide Manu VIII. 305. This sentiment is expressed even by a great poet like KÄlidÄsa106 ‘forest-dwellers give a sixth part of their tapas (merit due to austerities) to the king and that is an inexhaustible treasure’. It is further to be noted that not only Å›rotriyas, but also many other persons were free from taxation. Ä€p. Dh. S. (II. 10. 26. 11-17) exempts from taxation all woman of the four vará¹‡as, all boys before they show signs of manhood (i.e. before they attain majority), those who stay with their teachers for learning (even though they may be grown up), those engaged in austerities and devoted to right practices, a Å›Å«dra who washes the feet (of men of the three higher classes), the blind, the dumb and the diseased, those who are forbidden to possess wealth (i.e. ascetics etc.). The Vas. Dh. S. (19. 23)107 exempts from taxation the king’s servants, helpless [[145]] persons, ascetics, minors, senile men (above 70), young men, and women who are recently delivered. Manu VIII. 394 contains a similar provision. Bá¹›hat-ParÄÅ›ara108 (chap. III, Jiv. part II, p. 113) says ‘a brÄhmaá¹‡a engaged in agriculture had to pay nothing to anybody’. It is extremely doubtful whether in actual practice kings respected all these rules. Note 275 shows that a brÄhmaá¹‡a engaging in agriculture had to pay 1/6th of the produce just as others did. An inscription of VikramÄditya V, found near Gadag dated Å›ake 934 (1012 A.D.) refers to taxes levied even on upanayana, marriages and vedic sacrifices (E. I. vol. XX, pp. 64 and 70). The ÅšÄntiparva (76. 2-10) contains an interesting disquisition on the taxation of brÄhmaá¹‡as. Those brÄhmaá¹‡as who have mastered all the lores and who treat all equally well are called brahmasama. Those brÄhmaá¹‡as who have studied the á¹šgveda, Yajurveda and SÄmaveda and who stick to the peculiar duties of their class are styled devasama (verses 2 and 3). A religious king should make those who are not Å›rotriyas and who do not kindle the sacred fires render taxes and forced labour (verse 5). Then certain brÄhmaá¹‡as are described as ká¹£atrasama and vaiÅ›yasama. A king whose treasury is empty should levy taxes from all brÄhmaá¹‡as except those that are described as brahmasama and devasama.109 Even if these rules were honoured, the claim was not very excessive. The brÄhmaá¹‡as who ministered to the religious wants of the people and who were to conserve the religious literature and spiritual inheritance of the country and to teach without the liberty to make a contract for fees were never entitled to raise taxes from the people for their benefit. According to the practice of the Roman Catholic Church ’the clergy owed no allegiance to the secular power; they were not under the laws of the land, they paid no taxes to the State. All benefices were put under the Holy See and the Roman chancery compiled a tariff of prices for which each might be bought’. It would take too long to [[146]] enumerate the other exactions of the same kindâ€”the Tithes, Annates, Procurations, Subsidies and Dispensations.110

(6) In the matter of treasure trove the brÄhmaá¹‡a was more favourably treated than members of other classes. If a treasure was found by a learned brÄhmaá¹‡a he was entitled to keep the whole of it; in other cases the treasure belonged to the king, except a sixth part which was given to the finder if he honestly informed the king about the finding of the treasure. If a king himself found buried treasure, he was to distribute half of it among brÄhmaá¹‡as and was to keep for himself the other half. Vide Gautama (X. 43-45), Vas. Dh. S. (III. 13-14), Manu (VIII. 37-38), YÄj. II. 34-35, Viá¹£á¹‡u Dh. S. (III. 56-64), NÄrada (asvÄmivikraya verses 7-8).

(7) The general rule about the property of one dying heirless is that it escheats to the king, but there was an exception in the case of an heirless brÄhmaá¹‡a. Such property was to be distributed among Å›rotriyas or brÄhmaá¹‡as. Vide Gautama111 28. 39-40, Vas. Dh. S. 17. 84-87, Baudh. Dh. S. I. 5. 118-122, Manu IX. 188-189, Viá¹£á¹‡u Dh. BrÄhmaá¹‡a and precedence on a road

(8) The rule of the road was in favour of brÄhmaá¹‡as even as against the king. If on a road there was a crowd or obstruction, precedence was to be given to the cartman, to a very old man, to one suffering from a disease, to a woman, to a snÄtaka, to the king; but a king was to give precedence to a Å›rotriyaâ€”Gaut. VI. 21-22. Ä€p. adds that one carrying113 a burden should be given precedence and all who desire their own welfare should [[147]] give precedence to fools, patita, the intoxicated and lunatics and a person of a lower vará¹‡a should give precedence to one of a higher vará¹‡a. The MahÄbhÄrata (Vanaparva 133. 17) adds the blind and the deaf; (AnuÅ›Äsana 104. 25-26) cows, a pregnant woman and a weak man. Vas. Dh. S. Manu II. 138-139 has the same list and prefers the snÄtaka to the king; YÄj. I. 117 has the same rules. Åšaá¹…kha (quoted in the Mit. on YÄj. The MÄrkaá¹‡á¸eyapurÄá¹‡a (34. 39-41) has a long list which includes a prostitute and one who is an enemy. The Viá¹£á¹‡u Dh. S. (V. 91) prescribes a fine of 25 kÄrá¹£Äpaá¹‡as for him who does not give precedence on the road to one who deserves it. It will be conceded by every one that the above rules (except the one about the precedence of brÄhmaá¹‡as over even the king) are quite reasonable and are informed by a spirit of humanity and chivalry. The rule about learned brÄhmaá¹‡as probably owes its origin to the emphasis laid on the importance of the diffusion of learning (which was not the direct concern of the state in those days, but of the brÄhmaá¹‡as) and the superiority of knowledge over mere brute force or military achievements.

A question that very much exercised the minds of all {{smrtikÄras|smá¹›tikÄras}} and writers of digests116 was whether a brÄhmaá¹‡a who was himself guilty of violence or serious offences could be killed in self-defence by one attacked. The {{diota|dicta}} of the {{smÅ›tis|smá¹›tis}} are somewhat conflicting. Manu IV. 162 lays down a general rule prohibiting himsÄ (death or injury) of one’s teacher (of Veda), expounder (of the meaning of the Veda), one’s parents, one’s other teachers (or elders), brÄhmaá¹‡as, cows and all persons engaged in austerities. Manu XI. 89 lays down that there is no expiation ({{prayasoitta|prÄyaÅ›citta}}) that will wipe off the sin of intentionally killing a brÄhmaá¹‡a. But Manu himself (VIII. 350-351 = Viá¹£á¹‡u Dh. S. V. 189-190 = MatsyapurÄá¹‡a 227. 115-117 = Vá¹›ddha-HÄrÄ«ta IX. 349-350) says ‘one may surely kill without hesitation a man who comes down upon one as an ÄtatÄyin117 (a desperate character or violent man), whether he be a teacher, a child or an old man or a learned brÄhmaá¹‡a. In killing an ÄtatÄyin, the killer incurs no sin (or fault), whether he kills him in the presence of people or alone; (in such a case) wrath meets wrath’: Vas. Dh. S. (III. 15-18) expressly says ‘by killing an ÄtatÄyin they say the killer incurs no sin whatever’ and quotes three verses ‘an incendiary, a poisoner, one armed with a weapon, a robber, one who wrests a field or carries away one’s wifeâ€”these six are called ÄtatÄyin. When [[149]] an ÄtatÄyin comes (to attack) with the desire to kill (or harm), one may kill him even though he be a complete master of VedÄnta; by so doing one does not become a brÄhmaá¹‡a murderer. If a person kills one who has studied the Veda and who is born of a good family, because the latter is an ÄtatÄyin, he does not thereby become a brÄhmaá¹‡a-murderer as in that case fury meets fury.’118 In the ÅšÄntiparva (34. 17 and 19)119 we have similar verses “If a brÄhmaá¹‡a approaches wielding a weapon in a battle and desirous of killing a person, the latter may kill him even if the former be a complete master of the Veda. If a person kills a brÄhmaá¹‡a ÄtatÄyin who has swerved from right conduct, he does not thereby… … …fury’. {{Udyog& parya|Udyogaparva}} (178. 51-52) says that, if a man kills in a battle a brÄhmaá¹‡a who fights like a ká¹£atriya, it is a settled rule that he does not incur the sin of brÄhmaá¹‡a-murder. ÅšÄnti (22. 5-6) is to the same effect. The Viá¹£á¹‡u Dh. S. Sumantu as quoted by the Mit. (on YÄj. KÄtyÄyana121 (quoted in the Sm. Bá¹›haspati122 also says that he who would not kill a brÄhmaá¹‡a ÄtatÄyin [[150]] deserving to be killed for his violence would obtain the merit of an AÅ›vamedha sacrifice.

Commentators and writers of digests differ in their interpretations. ViÅ›varÅ«pa123 (on YÄj. III. 222) remarks that he is guilty of brÄhmaá¹‡a-murder who kills a brÄhmaá¹‡a except in battle or except when the latter is an ÄtatÄyin, or who kills a brÄhmaá¹‡a (not an ÄtatÄyin nor fighting) on his own account without being employed by another or who brings about the death of such a brÄhmaá¹‡a by hiring another to perpetrate the murder for money. He further adds that the man who kills a brÄhmaá¹‡a at the instigation of another for money is not guilty of the sin of brÄhmaá¹‡a-murder, but it is the instigator who is so guilty on the analogy of the rule that the merit or fruit of a sacrifice belongs to him on whose behalf the á¹›tviks perform it. The Mit.124 on YÄj. The final conclusion of the Mit. is that if a brÄhmaá¹‡a who is an ÄtatÄyin is being opposed in self-defence without any desire to kill him and if he dies through mistake or inattention, then the killer incurs no punishment at the hand of the king and has to undergo a slight prÄyaÅ›citta i.e. there is really a prohibition to kill an ÄtatÄyin brÄhmaá¹‡a and verses like Manu VIII. 351 refer to an ÄtatÄyin who is not a brÄhmaá¹‡a. MedhÄtithi appears to have held the same view (on Manu VIII. 350-351). KullÅ«ka explains Manu VIII. 350 as meaning that ‘a guru or a brÄhmaá¹‡a or others coming as ÄtatÄyins may be killed when it is impossible to save oneself even by fleeing from them’. AparÄrka is of opinion125 [[151]] that where an ÄtatÄyin brÄhmaá¹‡a cannot be prevented from his wicked intent except by killing him, there only the Å›Ästras allow the killing of a brÄhmaá¹‡a but where it is possible to ward him off by a mere blow (i.e. without actually killing him) there would be the sin of brÄhmaá¹‡a-murder if he were actually killed. The Sm. The VyavahÄra-MayÅ«kha126 adds a rider that, on account of the prescription contained in the section on kalivarjya (actions forbidden in the Kali age) viz. ’the killing in a properly conducted fight of brÄhmaá¹‡as that are ÄtatÄyin’ (is forbidden in Kali), an ÄtatÄyin brÄhmaá¹‡a even when about to kill a person should not be killed by that person in the Kali age, that such a brÄhmaá¹‡a was allowed to be killed in former ages, that an ÄtatÄyin brÄhmaá¹‡a other than one bent upon killing another was not to be killed in all ages. The VÄ«ramitrodaya (pp. 19-27) has a long disquisition on this subject but space forbids us from giving even a brief summary of it. It will have been noticed how the sacredness of the brÄhmaá¹‡a’s person went on increasing in later ages.

(10) Even threatening a brÄhmaá¹‡a with assault, or striking him or drawing blood from his body drew the severest condemnation from very ancient times. The Tai.127 S. (11) For certain offences a brÄhmaá¹‡a received lesser punishment than members of other classes. (12) According to Gaut.131 (XIII. 4) a brÄhmaá¹‡a could not be cited as a witness by a litigant who was not a brÄhmaá¹‡a and the king would not summon him, provided he (the brÄhmaá¹‡a) was not an attesting witness on a document. Minor privileges of brÄhmaá¹‡as

(13) Only certain brÄhmaá¹‡as were to be invited for dinner in Å›rÄddhas and in rites for gods. Vide Gaut. 15. 5 and 9, Ä€p. Dh. S. II. 7. 17. 4, Manu III. 124 and 128, YÄj. I. 217, 219, 221. [[153]]

Disabilities of Å›Å«dras

It may be convenient to bring together the disabilities of the Å›Å«dra:

(1) He was not allowed to study the Veda. Many of the smá¹›tikÄras and writers of digests137 quote several Vedic passages on this point. A Å›ruti text reads ‘(The Creator) created the brÄhmaá¹‡a with GÄyatrÄ« (metre), the rÄjanya with Triá¹£á¹­ubh, the vaiÅ›ya with JagatÄ«, but he did not create the Å›Å«dra with any metre; therefore the Å›Å«dra is known to be unfit for the saá¹ƒskÄra (of upanayana)’. The study of the Veda follows after Upanayana and the Veda speaks of the Upanayana of only three classes138 ‘one should perform upanayana for a brÄhmaá¹‡a in spring, for a rÄjanya in summer and in Å›arad (autumn) for a vaiÅ›ya.’ Not only was the Å›Å«dra not to study the Veda, but Veda study was not to be carried on in his presence (vide note 75 above).139 This attitude need not cause wonder. The sacred Vedic literature was largely created and preserved entirely by the brÄhmaá¹‡as (the ká¹£atriyas contributing if at all a very small share in that task). If the brÄhmaá¹‡as desired to keep their sacred treasure for the twice-born classes in these circumstances, it is understandable and for those ages even excusable. In the 20th century there are vast majorities who are not allowed by small minorities of imperialistic and capitalistic tendencies to control the just and equitable distribution of the material goods produced mostly by the labour and co-operation of those majorities and doctrines are being openly professed that certain races alone should be imparted higher and scientific knowledge while other so-called inferior races should be only hewers of wood and drawers of water.

There are however faint traces that in ancient times this prohibition of Veda study was not so absolute and universal as the smá¹›tis make it. In the ChÄndogya Upaniá¹£ad IV. 1-2, we have the story of JÄnaÅ›ruti PautrÄyana and Raikva where the latter addresses JÄnaÅ›ruti as Å›Å«dra and imparts to him the Saá¹ƒvarga (absorption) vidyÄ.140 It appears that JÄnaÅ›ruti was a Å›Å«dra to whom the vidyÄ embodied in the ChÄndogya (which is also Veda) was imparted. It is no doubt true that in the VedÄntasÅ«tra141 (I. 3. 34) the word Å›Å«dra is explained not as referring to the class, but as meaning that sorrow (Å›uc) arose in JÄnaÅ›ruti on hearing the contemptuous talk of the flamingoes about himself and he was overcome (from dru) by that (i.e. Å›Å«dra is derived from Å›uc and dru). But this far-fetched explanation had to be given because of the practice current in the times of the VedÄntasÅ«tras that the Å›Å«dra is not entitled to study the Veda. Gaut. XII. 4 went so far as to prescribe ‘if the Å›Å«dra intentionally listens for committing to memory the Veda, then his ears should be filled with (molten) lead and lac; if he utters the Veda, then his tongue may be cut off; if he has mastered the Veda his body should be hacked’.142

Though the Å›Å«dra could not study the Veda, he was not debarred from hearing the itihÄsas (like the MahÄbhÄrata) and the PurÄá¹‡as. The143 MahÄbhÄrata (ÅšÄnti 328. 49) expressly says that the four vará¹‡as should hear the MahÄbhÄrata through a brÄhmaá¹‡a as reader. The BhÄgavatapurÄá¹‡a144 says that as the three Vedas cannot be learnt by women, Å›Å«dras and brÄhmaá¹‡as (who are so only by birth), the sage (VyÄsa) composed the story of the BhÄrata out of compassion for them. The ÅšÅ«drakamalÄkara145 (pp. 13-14) cites several passages from the purÄá¹‡as [[156]] to the effect that the Å›Å«dra could not study the smá¹›tis and purÄá¹‡as by himself. Even Manu II. 16 seems to suggest that only the dvijÄtis had the privilege to listen to the Manusmá¹›ti (and not Å›Å«dras). The only privilege conceded by the ÅšÅ«drakamalÄkara to the Å›Å«dra is that he can acquire knowledge by listening to the purÄá¹‡as read by a brÄhmaá¹‡a (p. 17); the Kalpataru and other works allowed the Å›Å«dra to read and repeat PurÄá¹‡a mantras. Åšaá¹…karÄcÄrya on VedÄntasÅ«tra (I. 3. 38) quotes ÅšÄnti 328. 49 and says that the Å›Å«dra has no adhikÄra (eligibility) for brahmavidyÄ based upon a study of the Veda, but that a Å›Å«dra can attain spiritual development (just as Vidura and DharmavyÄdha mentioned in the MahÄbhÄrata did) and that he may attain to moká¹£a, the fruit of correct knowledge. In certain digests we find a smá¹›ti quotation to the effect that Å›Å«dras are VÄjasaneyins. This is146 explained as meaning that the Å›Å«dra should follow the procedure prescribed in the gá¹›hyasÅ«tra of the VÄjasaneya ÅšÄkhÄ and a brÄhmaá¹‡a should repeat the mantra for him. This is probably based on the Harivaá¹ƒÅ›a (Bhaviá¹£yat-parva, chap. ÅšÅ«dras not authorized for Vedic rites

(2) The Å›Å«dras were not to consecrate sacred fires and to perform the solemn Vedic sacrifices. Vide note 73 above. Jaimini (I. 3. 25-38) elaborately discusses this question and arrives at the conclusion that the Å›Å«dra cannot consecrate the three sacred fires and so cannot perform Vedic rites. Among the reasons given are that in several Vedic passages only the three higher classes are referred to in the case of the consecration of fires, about the sÄmans to be sung, about the food to be taken when observing vrata.147 It is however interesting to note that at least one ancient teacher (BÄdari)148 was found who advocated that [[157]] even Å›Å«dras could perform Vedic sacrifices. The BhÄradvÄja Åšrauta-sÅ«tra149 (V. 2. 8) states the opinion of some that the Å›Å«dra can consecrate the three sacred Vedic fires. The KÄtyÄyana-Å›rautasÅ«tra (I. 4. 5) prescribes that all can perform Vedic rites except those who are deficient in a limb, who are not learned in the150 Veda, who are impotent and Å›Å«dras; but the commentary thereon states by way of pÅ«rvapaká¹£a that there are certain Vedic texts which lead to the inference that the Å›Å«dra had the adhikÄra for Vedic rites e.g. in Åšat. Br. I. 1. 4. 12 (S. B. E. vol. XII, p. 28) it is said with reference to the Haviá¹£ká¹›t call “Now there are four different forms of this call, viz. ‘Come hither’ (ehi) in the case of a brÄhmaá¹‡a; ‘approach’ (Ägahi) and ‘hasten hither’ (Ädrava) in the case of a vaiÅ›ya and a member of the military caste and ‘run hither’ (ÄdhÄva) in that of a Å›Å«dra.” Similarly in the SomayÄga in place of the payovrata (vow to drink milk only) mastu (whey) is prescribed for Å›Å«dra (indicating thereby that the Å›Å«dra could perform a SomayÄga) and in Åšat. Br. (XIII. 8. 3. 11, S. B. E, vol. 44, p. 435) with reference to sepulchral mounds it is said ‘for the ká¹£atriya he may make it as high as a man with upstretched arms, for a brÄhmaá¹‡a reaching up to the mouth, for a woman up to the hips, for a vaiÅ›ya up to the thighs, for a Å›Å«dra up to the knee’. The commentary on the KÄtyÄyana-Åšrauta I. 1. 6 says that the word Å›Å«dra here stands for rathakÄra because (acc. to YÄj. I. 95) his mother’s mother is a Å›Å«dra woman.

Though the Å›Å«dra was not authorized to perform Vedic rites, he was entitled to perform what is called pÅ«rta-dharma151 i.e. the building of wells, tanks, temples, parks and distribution of food as works of charity and gifts on such occasions as eclipses and the Sun’s passage from one zodiacal sign into another and on the 12th and other tithis. He was allowed to perform the five daily sacrifices called MahÄyajÃ±as [[158]] in the ordinary fire, he could perform Å›rÄddha, he was to think of the devatÄs and utter loudly the word ’namaá¸¥’ which was to be the only mantra in his case (i.e. he was not to say ‘Agnaye svÄhÄ’ but to think of Agni and say[^371] ’namaá¸¥’). Manu X. 127 prescribes that all religious rites for the Å›Å«dra are without (Vedic) mantras. According to some the Å›Å«dra could also have what is called VaivÄhika fire (i.e. fire kindled at the time of marriage) in Manu III. 67 and YÄj. I. 97, but MedhÄtithi (on the same verse), the Mit. (on YÄj. I. 121), the MadanapÄrijÄta (p. 231) and other works say that he should offer oblations in the ordinary fire and that there is no VaivÄhika fire for the Å›Å«dra. All persons including the Å›Å«dras and even cÄá¹‡á¸Älas were authorized to repeat the RÄmamantra of 13 letters (ÅšrÄ« RÄma jaya RÄma jaya jaya RÄma) and the Åšiva mantra of five letters (namaá¸¥ Å›ivÄya), while dvijÄtis could repeat the Åšiva mantra of six letters (Oá¹ƒ namaá¸¥ Å›ivÄya). Vide ÅšÅ«dra-kamalÄkara pp. 30-31, where passages of VarÄha, VÄmana and Bhaviá¹£ya PurÄá¹‡as are cited to show that Å›Å«dras are entitled to learn and repeat mantras of Viá¹£á¹‡u from the PaÃ±carÄtra texts and of Åšiva, the Sun, Åšakti and VinÄyaka. The VarÄhapurÄá¹‡a (128. 22-31) describes the initiation (dÄ«ká¹£Ä) of a Å›Å«dra as a devotee of Viá¹£á¹‡u (as a bhÄgavata).

The saá¹ƒskÄras of Å›Å«dras

(3) As to {{Sanskaras|Saá¹ƒskÄras}}, there is some apparent conflict among the authorities. Manu X. 126 says “The Å›Å«dra incurs no sin (by eating forbidden articles like onions and garlic), he is not fit for saá¹ƒskÄras, he has no adhikÄra for (authority to perform) dharma nor is he forbidden from performing dharma’ and in IV. 80 (which is the same as Vas. Dh. S. 18. 14 and Viá¹£á¹‡u Dh. S. 71. 48-52) we see ‘one should not give advice to a Å›Å«dra, nor give him leavings of food nor of sacrificial oblations, one [[159]] should not impart religious instruction to him nor ask him to perform vratas’. Laghuviá¹£á¹‡u152 (I. 15) contains the dictum that the Å›Å«dra is devoid of any saá¹ƒskÄra. The Mit. on YÄj. III. 262 explains the words of Manu IV. 80 about vratas in the case of Å›Å«dras as applicable only to those Å›Å«dras who are not in attendance upon members of the three higher castes and establishes that Å›Å«dras can perform vratas (but without homa and muttering of mantras). AparÄrka on the same verse (Manu IV. 80) explains that the Å›Å«dra cannot perform vratas in person, but only through the medium of a brÄhmaá¹‡a. The ÅšÅ«drakamalÄkara (p. 38) holds that Å›Å«dras are entitled to perform vratas, fasts, mahÄdÄnas and prÄyaÅ›cittas, but without homa and japa. Manu X. 127 allows religious Å›Å«dras to perform all religious acts which dvijÄtis perform, provided they do not use Vedic mantras. On the other hand Åšaá¹…kha (as quoted by ViÅ›varÅ«pa on YÄj. I. 13) opines that saá¹ƒskÄras may be performed for Å›Å«dras but without Vedic mantras. Yama quoted in Sm. C. (I. p. 14) says the same. Veda-VyÄsa (I. 17) prescribes that ten saá¹ƒskÄras (viz. garbhÄdhÄna, puá¹ƒsavana, sÄ«mantonnayana, jÄtakarma, nÄmakaraá¹‡a, niá¹£kramaá¹‡a, annaprÄÅ›ana, caula, kará¹‡avedha and vivÄha) can be performed in the case of Å›Å«dras, but without Vedic mantras. HÄradatta (on Gautama X. 51) quotes a gá¹›hyakÄra to the effect that even in the case of the Å›Å«dra the rites of niá¹£eka, puá¹ƒsavana, sÄ«mantonnayana, jÄtakarma, nÄmakaraá¹‡a, annaprÄÅ›ana and caula are allowed but without Vedic mantras. When Manu prescribes (II. 32) that the Å›Å«dra should be given a name connected with service, he indicates that the Å›Å«dra could perform the ceremony of nÄmakaraá¹‡a. So when Manu (IV. 80) states that he deserves no saá¹ƒskÄra, what he means is that no saá¹ƒskÄra with Vedic mantras was to be performed in his case. MedhÄtithi on Manu IV. 80 says that the prohibition to give advice and impart instruction in dharma applies only when these are done for making one’s livelihood, but if a Å›Å«dra is a friend of the family of a brÄhmaá¹‡a friendly advice or instruction can be given. Vide ÅšÅ«drakamalÄkara p. 47 for several views about the saá¹ƒskÄras allowed to Å›Å«dras.

(4) Liability to higher punishment for certain offences. If a Å›Å«dra committed adultery with a woman of the three [[160]] higher castes, Gaut.153 (XII. 1-2) prescribed the cutting off of his penis and forfeiture of all his property and if he was guilty of this offence when entrusted with the duty of protecting her, he was to suffer death in addition. Vaá¹£. Dh. S. 21. 1. Manu VIII. 366 prescribes death in the case of a Å›Å«dra having intercourse with a brÄhmaá¹‡a woman whether she was willing or unwilling. On the other hand, if a brÄhmaá¹‡a committed rape on a brÄhmaá¹‡a woman he was fined a thousand and five hundred if he was guilty of adultery with her (Manu VIII. 378) and if a brÄhmaá¹‡a had intercourse with a ká¹£atriya, vaiÅ›ya or Å›Å«dra woman, who was not guarded, he was fined five hundred (Manu VIII. 385). Similarly in the case of VÄkpÄruá¹£ya (slander and libel) if a Å›Å«dra reviled a brÄhmaá¹‡a he received corporal punishment or his tongue was cut off (Manu VIII. 270), but if a ká¹£atriya or vaiÅ›ya did so they were respectively fined 100 or 150 (Manu VIII. 267) and if a brÄhmaá¹‡a reviled a Å›Å«dra, the brÄhmaá¹‡a was fined only 12 (Manu VIII. 268) or nothing (acc. to Gaut. XII. 10). In the case of theft, however, the Å›Å«dra was fined much less. Vide above p. 152 (No. 11 among the privileges of brÄhmaá¹‡as).

(5) In the matter of the period for impurity on death or birth the Å›Å«dra was held to be impure for a month, while a brÄhmaá¹‡a had to observe a ten days’ period only. Vide above p. 153 (No. 15 among the privileges of brÄhmaá¹‡as).

Disabilities of Å›Å«dras

(6) A Å›Å«dra could not be a judge or propound what dharma was. Manu (VIII. 9) and YÄj. I. 3 lay down that when the king does not himself look into the litigation of people owing to pressure of other business, he should appoint a learned brÄhmaá¹‡a as a judge. Manu (VIII. 20) further says that a king may appoint as his judge even a brÄhmaá¹‡a who is so by birth only (i.e. who does not perform the peculiar duties of brÄhmaá¹‡as), but never a Å›Å«dra. KÄtyÄyana (as quoted [[161]] in the Mit. on YÄj. I. 3) says that when a brÄhmaá¹‡a is not available (as a judge) the king may appoint as judge a ká¹£atriya or a vaiÅ›ya who is proficient in dharmaÅ›Ästra, but he should carefully avoid appointing a Å›Å«dra as judge.154

(7) A brÄhmaá¹‡a was not allowed to receive gifts from a Å›Å«dra except under great restrictions. Vide above note 239.

(8) A brÄhmaá¹‡a could take155 food at the houses of members of the three classes who performed the duties prescribed for them by the Å›Ästras (according to Gaut.), but he could not take food from a Å›Å«dra except when the Å›Å«dra was his own cowherd, or tilled his field or was a hereditary friend of the family, or his own barber or his dÄsa. Vide Gaut. XVII. 6 and Manu IV. 253 (=Viá¹£á¹‡u Dh. S. 57. 16), YÄj. I. 166, ParÄÅ›ara IX. 19. Ä€p. Dh. S. I. 5. 16. 22 says156 ’that food which is brought by an impure Å›Å«dra should not be eaten by a brÄhmaá¹‡a;’ but Ä€pastamba allows Å›Å«dras to be cooks in brÄhmaá¹‡a households provided they were supervised by a member of the three higher classes and observed certain hygienic rules about paring nails, the cutting of hair. Manu IV. 211 forbade in general the food of a Å›Å«dra to a brÄhmaá¹‡a and by IV. 223 he laid down that a learned brÄhmaá¹‡a should not take cooked food from a Å›Å«dra who did not perform Å›rÄddha and other daily rites (mahÄyajÃ±as) but that he may take from such a Å›Å«dra uncooked grain for one night, if he cannot get food from anywhere else. Baudh. Dh. S. (II. 2. 1) requires a brÄhmaá¹‡a to avoid the food of vá¹›á¹£alas (Å›Å«dras). Gradually rules about taking food from Å›Å«dras became stricter. The Åšaá¹…kha-smá¹›ti (13. 4) remarks that brÄhmaá¹‡as fattened on the food given by Å›Å«dras are paá¹…ktidÅ«á¹£aka. ParÄÅ›ara157 XI. 13 ordains that a brÄhmaá¹‡a may take from a Å›Å«dra ghee, oil, milk, molasses and food fried in oil or ghee, but should eat it on a river bank and not in the Å›Å«dra’s house and the Par. M. adds [[162]] (II. 1, pp. 411-12) that this permission is meant to apply only when the brÄhmaá¹‡a is tired by travelling and no food from a member of another class is available. HÄradatta on Gaut. XVII. 6 remarks that a brÄhmaá¹‡a could take food from a Å›Å«dra who was a cowherd &c. only in the case of very extreme calamities. AparÄrka also (p. 244 on YÄj. I. 168) says the same. In the kalivarjya (actions forbidden in the kali age) the old practice of eating the food of cowherds, barber &c. was forbidden.158

(9) The Å›Å«dra gradually came to be so much looked down upon that he could not touch a brÄhmaá¹‡a, though at one time he could be a cook in a brÄhmaá¹‡a household and a brÄhmaá¹‡a could eat food from his house. In the AnuÅ›Äsanaparva (59. 33) it is said159 ‘a brÄhmaá¹‡a should be served by a Å›Å«dra from a distance like blazing fire while he may be waited upon by a ká¹£atriya or vaiÅ›ya after touching him.’ AparÄrka (p. 1196) quotes two smá¹›ti texts ‘a brÄhmaá¹‡a on touching a Å›Å«dra or niá¹£Äda becomes pure by Äcamana (ceremonial sipping of water); on touching persons lower than these, he becomes pure by bathing, prÄá¹‡ÄyÄma and the strength of tapas; on seeing a ram, a cock, a crow, a dog, a Å›Å«dra and an antyÄvasÄyin (an antyaja), one should stop the rite that is being performed and on touching them one should take a bath’. On this AparÄrka explains that if a man who touched a Å›Å«dra cannot bathe then he may resort to sipping water, but if able he must take a bath or that on touching a sat-Å›Å«dra one may have recourse to Äcamana and on touching an asat-Å›Å«dra one must take a bath. We find from the Gá¹›hyasÅ«tras160 that in Madhuparka offered to a snÄtaka the feet of the guest (even if he was a brÄhmaá¹‡a) were washed by a Å›Å«dra male or female. So there could have been no ban against a Å›Å«dra touching a brÄhmaá¹‡a then. The Ä€p. Dh. S. (II. 3. 6. 9-10) says that two Å›Å«dras should wash the feet of a guest, according to some teachers (in the case of a householder who has several dÄsas), while Ä€pastamba [[163]] himself says that one Å›Å«dra should wash the guest’s feet and another should sprinkle him with water.161

(10) As the Å›Å«dra could not be initiated into Vedic study, the only ÄÅ›rama out of the four that he was entitled to was that of the householder. In the AnuÅ›Äsanaparva (165. 10) we read162 ‘I am a Å›Å«dra and so I have no right to resort to the four ÄÅ›ramas’. In the ÅšÄntiparva163 (63. 12-14) it is said, ‘in the case of a Å›Å«dra who performs service (of the higher classes), who has done his duty, who has raised offspring, who has only a short span of life left or is reduced to the 10th stage (i.e. is above 90 years of age), the fruits of all ÄÅ›ramas are laid down (as obtained by him) except of the fourth.’ MedhÄtithi on Manu VI. 97 explains these words as meaning that the Å›Å«dra by serving brÄhmaá¹‡as and procreating offspring as a householder acquires the merit of all ÄÅ›ramas except moká¹£a which is the reward of the proper observance of the duties of the fourth ÄÅ›rama.

(11) The life of a Å›Å«dra was esteemed rather low. YÄj. III. 236 and Manu XI. 66 include the killing of a woman, a Å›Å«dra, a vaiÅ›ya and a ká¹£atriya among upapÄtakas; but the prÄyaÅ›cittas and gifts prescribed for killing these show that the life of the Å›Å«dra was not worth much. On killing a ká¹£atriya, the prÄyaÅ›citta prescribed was brahmacarya for six years, gift of 1000 cows and a bull; for killing a vaiÅ›ya, brahmacarya for three years and gift of 100 cows and a bull; for killing a Å›Å«dra brahmacarya for one year, gift of 10 cows and a bull. Gaut. XXII. 14-16, Manu XI. 128-130, YÄj. III. 266-267 say practically the same thing. Ä€p. Dh. S. (I. 9. 25. 14-I. 9. 26. 1) says that on killing a crow, a chameleon, a peacock, a cakravÄka, flamingo, bhÄsa, a frog, ichneumon, muskrat, a dog, a cow and draught ox the prÄyaÅ›citta is the same as that for killing a Å›Å«dra. Manu (XI. 131) says ‘on killing a cat, an ichneumon, [[164]] cÄá¹£a, a frog, a dog, iguana, owl and crow, the prÄyaÅ›citta is the same as that for killing a Å›Å«dra.’164

If the Å›Å«dra laboured under certain grave disabilities, he had certain compensating advantages. He could follow almost any profession except the few specially reserved for brÄhmaá¹‡as and ká¹£atriyas. Even as to the latter many Å›Å«dras became kings and Kauá¹­ilya in his ArthaÅ›Ästra (IX. 2) speaks of armies of Å›Å«dras (vide note 266 above). The Å›Å«dra was free from the round of countless daily rites. He was compelled to undergo no saá¹ƒskÄra (except marriage), he could indulge in any kind of food and drink wine, he had to undergo no penances for lapses from the rules of the Å›Ästras, he had to observe no restrictions of gotra and pravara in marriage. Those western writers who turn up their nose at the position of the Å›Å«dras in ancient and medieval India conveniently forget what atrocious crimes were perpetrated by their people in the institution of slavery and in their dealings with the Red Indians and other backward coloured races; how nations of Europe out of false pride of race have passed in the 20th century laws prohibiting marriages between the so-called Ä€ryans and non-Ä€ryans and preventing the latter from holding state offices and carrying on several occupations and how discrimination is made against coloured men on railways, in hotels and other places of public resort and how even in India separate third class compartments were reserved on railways for Europeans, for entering which Indians were prosecuted and sentenced in their own country. Vide Emperor vs. Narayan 25 Bom. L. R. 26 for such a case.

  5. Vide Deussen’s ‘Das System des Vedanta’, 1883, (pp. 18-19) ’the real cherisher of these thoughts was originally the caste of the ká¹£atriyas, rather than the caste of the priests. Over and over again we come across the situation that the brÄhmaá¹‡a asks the ká¹£atriya for information’ and Deussen refers only to six passages (Bá¹›. Up. II. 1, VI. 2, Ch. Up. V. 3 and V. 11 and Kauá¹£Ä«taki Up. I. 1 and IV. 1). Vide also ‘Philosophy of the Upaniá¹£ads’ (translated by Geden, 1905, pp. 17-19). In the first place these are too few passages out of the vast Upaniá¹£ad literature to found the sweeping generalisation in which the German savant indulges. In the second place in Bá¹›. Up. II. 1 and Kauá¹£Ä«taki Up. IV there is no statement that brahmavidyÄ was known only to ká¹£atriyas; on the contrary AjÄtaÅ›atru expresses surprise that a brÄhmaá¹‡a should approach a ká¹£atriya for the expounding of brahmavidyÄ and says that this is opposed to the natural (or usual) order of affairs. This shows that AjÄtaÅ›atru was an exception and that brÄhmaá¹‡as usually taught brahmavidyÄ. In Kauá¹£Ä«taki I. 1 and Ch. Up. V. 11 all that is narrated is that Gautama Åšvetaketu learnt from Citra GÄrgyÄyaá¹‡i and certain Å›rotriyas like Aupamanyava learnt VaiÅ›vÄnara vidyÄ from AÅ›vapati Kaikeya. But nothing is said here about brahmavidyÄ being first known to ká¹£atriyas only. In Bá¹›. Up. VI. 2 and Ch. Up. V. 3 it is no doubt stated that ’this VidyÄ’ was not known to any brÄhmaá¹‡a till then; but ’this vidyÄ’ does not mean the whole of the philosophy of brahma, but only that particular doctrine which bears the name of paÃ±cÄgnividyÄ. This vidyÄ no doubt propounds the doctrine of transmigration in a figurative and somewhat picturesque way. But that doctrine is elsewhere elaborated by brÄhmaá¹‡as like YÄjÃ±avalkya to the brÄhmaá¹‡as in king Janaka’s court and to Janaka himself (vide Bá¹›. Up. III. 2. 13 and IV. 4. 3-4). Nor can it be said that the doctrine of transmigration was not at all known before the Upaniá¹£ads. The same views are echoed by Sir R. G. Bhandarkar in ‘Verhandlungen der VII Internationalen Orientalisten Congresses zu Wien’ (arische Sec. pp. 108-109) and in ‘{{Vaippa vism and Saiyigm|Vaiá¹£á¹‡avism and Åšaivism}}’ p. 9 “Ká¹£atriyas engaged themselves in active speculation on religious matters about the time of the Upaniá¹£ads and are mentioned as the original possessors of the new knowledge,” and the learned Doctor refers only to Ch. Up. V. 3 and V. 11. It may be stated that Hopkins (in ‘Ethics of India’ 1924 p. 63), Barth (‘Religions of India’ p. 65) and Vedic Index (vol. II p. 206) do not subscribe to these views of Deussen and Bhandarkar. ↩︎

  17. The words ‘kusÅ«la’ and ‘kumbhÄ«’ have been variously explained by the commentators; vide KullÅ«ka on Manu IV. 7. According to KullÅ«ka one who has corn sufficient for three years is called “kusÅ«ladhÄnya” as suggested by Manu X. 7; while “kumbhÄ«dhÄnya” is one who has a store of corn for one year. MedhÄtithi says that there is no restriction to corn only; one who has wealth either in corn or money to satisfy his needs for three years is ‘kusÅ«ladhÄnya’; according to GovindarÄja, ‘kusÅ«ladhÄnya’ and ‘kumbhÄ«dhÄnya’ are respectively those who have corn for 12 and 6 days. The Mit. on YÄj. I. 128 accepts GovindarÄja’s explanation. ↩︎

  83. This is a mere arthavÄda. It means ‘it is as if his’, for Manu himself (in VIII. 338) prescribes for brÄhmaá¹‡as who knowing everything are guilty of theft 64 or 100 or 128 times as much fine, as a Å›Å«dra guilty of the same theft has to pay if he unknowingly commits it, while the latter pays eight times as much if he knowingly commits it. ↩︎

  164.
